The  Distance  Between  Floors  Heritage  Threads,  Gomti  Nagar,  Lucknow  —  March  15–16,  2026   ━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━   PART  1  —  Sunday,  March  15  |  Subah   Pihu's  room  first.   She  was  attempting  physics.   Pink  frock,  one  hand  on  the  floor,  one  gripping  the  table  leg  —  the  teddy  bear  was  six  inches

  Attempt  seven:  stretch  further.  Fail.  Recalibrate.  Attempt  eight:  different  angle.  Closer.  Almost  —   She  lunged.   The  teddy  came  free.  She  sat  down  hard,  surprised  by  her  own  success,  and  looked  at  it  like  
it
 
owed
 
her

  Then  she  looked  at  me.   "Aaa."   "Yeah,"  I  said.  "You  got  it."   She  bonked  the  teddy  against  the  table.  Twice.  Then  once  more  —  not  celebration,

Assistants.  Rajesh  Sir.   The  room  went  quiet.   Three  people  remained.   Nandini  Ji  settled  back  into  her  corner  —  dupatta  back  in  her  hands,  that  same  listening  
touch.

  One  second.  Naina's  file  stayed  exactly  where  it  was.   Then,  quietly  —  to  her  mother  —   "Inevitable."   Nandini  Ji  looked  up.  "Kya  matlab?"   Naina  looked  at  her  —  that  steady,  careful  look.   "Matlab  —  jo  hona  hai  woh  hoga.  Rok  nahi  sakte.  Cheezein  apni  jagah  find  kar  leti  hain.

I  knew  she  knew.   Neither  of  us  said  anything.   Then  she  looked  back  at  her  mother.   I  turned  toward  the  stairs.   ━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━   Downstairs.  Blue  rack.   Tag.  Fold.  Space.   Murli  had  gone  home.  Anshika  was  locking  the  counter.   I  folded.   That  word  was  still  in  the  room  somewhere  —  in  the  air  above  the  blue  rack,  in  the  space

Baba's  process  —  in  for  four,  hold,  out.  When  the  mind  wanders,  begin  again.   Inevitable.   The  word  sat  there.  Wouldn't  move.  Wouldn't  go  deeper,  wouldn't  surface. Inevitable.   ━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━   INTERLUDE  III  Naina's  Diary  March  17,  2026  —  Raat   ━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━   Maine  ek  photo  li  aaj.   Sirf  ek. Sachi  Bhaav

 Five  months  later,  the  illusion  of  the  typical  North-Indian  "new  baby"  phase  evaporated.

wealth.
 
"Bhaiya,  ek  chai,"  she  commanded  Chacha.  An  order,  not  a  request.  She  paid  a  ten-rupee  
note,
 
shoved
 
the
 
three
 
rupees
 
change
 
into
 
her
 
pocket
 
without
 
looking,
 
and
 
then
 
searched
 
for
 
a
 
lighter.
 
Click.  Click.  Click.  Nothing.  
"Lighter  kaam  nahi  kar  raha."  She  looked  at  me.  Direct,  brown  eyes.  Slightly  annoyed.  The  
first
 
time
 
she
 
actually
 
looked
 
at
 
me.
 
"Jala
 
sakte
 
ho?"
 
I  pulled  out  my  lighter.  The  flame  flickered.  She  stepped  close—two  steps—and  leaned  in.  
The
 
tip
 
of
 
her
 
cigarette
 
caught
 
the
 
flame.
 
She
 
took
 
a
 
deep
 
drag,
 
and
 
as
 
she
 
exhaled,
 
the
 
smoke
 
blew
 
straight
 
into
 
my
 
face.
 
I  coughed  lightly.  She  looked  at  me,  completely  cold  and  detached.  There  was  no  apology.  
She
 
couldn't
 
have
 
cared
 
less
 
where
 
the
 
smoke
 
went.
 
"Smoking  nahi  karte?"  she  asked  casually,  holding  her  phone.  
"Nahi.  Allergy  hai,"  I  replied.  
"Oh."  Just  one  word.  She  cut  the  conversation  right  there.  
"Quant  class  ke  liye  aaye  ho?"  she  asked  after  a  moment.  
"Nahi.  Library.  Isi  building  mein."  
"Charges?"  
"1300  monthly."  
"Okay.  Not  bad."  
We  finished  our  tea  and  started  walking  toward  the  building  together.  It  wasn't  planned.  It  just  
happened.
 
"Prep  kya  hai  tumhara?"  she  asked.  
"SSC  CGL."  "SSC...  matlab  UPSC  ke  under?"  
I  offered  an  automatic,  tired  smile.  "Nahi.  Alag  hai.  Staff  Selection  Commission.  UPSC  sirf  
IAS-IPS
 
ke
 
liye
 
hota
 
hai.
 
SSC
 
mein
 
taxes,
 
customs,
 
audit,
 
statistics
 
—
 
bahut
 
kuch.
 
Basically
 
desh
 
sirf
 
IAS
 
se
 
nahi
 
chalta,
 
poore
 
system
 
se
 
chalta
 
hai."
 
She  paused.  And  then,  breaking  through  her  arrogant  facade,  a  genuine  smile  appeared.  
"True.  Makes  sense  actually."  
We  parted  ways  at  the  gate.  I  went  down  to  the  basement.  Seat  17.  But  as  I  opened  my  
books,
 
something
 
wouldn't
 
settle.
 
Maybe
 
it
 
was
 
the
 
smoke
 
that
 
brushed
 
my
 
face,
 
or
 
maybe
 
it
 
was
 
that
 
'makes
 
sense
 
actually'
—a
 
rare,
 
unperformed
 
moment
 
of
 
genuine
 
acknowledgment.
 
It
 
was
 
a
 
five-minute
 
conversation.
 
I  looked  back  down  at  her  diary.  The  entry  for  that  same  night.  
10  December  2022,  10:52  PM  
Class  was  fine...  There  was  a  guy  at  the  tapri  this  morning.  Library  wala.  Lighter  kaam  nahi  
kar
 
raha
 
tha.
 
Usne
 
jala
 
diya.
 
Bas
 
itna.
 
Talked
 
for
 
maybe
 
5
 
minutes
 
—
 
SSC,
 
library
 
charges.
 
Normal.
 
But  one  thing.  When  I  asked  about  UPSC  he  didn't  get  irritated.  Didn't  condescend.  Just  said  
it
 
straight.
 
'Desh
 
IAS
 
se
 
nahi
 
chalta,
 
poore
 
system
 
se.'
 
Interesting

People  usually  assume  that  when  I  don't  know  something,  I  don't  care.  He  didn't  do  that.  
Don't
 
know

Don't
 
need

honestly.
 
Library
 
wala.
 
Shayad
 
phir

shayad

I  closed  her  diary  and  put  my  pen  down.  
Dono  ne  ek  doosre  ka  naam  nahi  jaana  tha.  We  both  thought  it  was  nothing.  Just  one  day.  
Just
 
five
 
minutes
 
that
 
would
 
pass
 
and

Dono  galat  the.  We  were  both  so  incredibly  wrong.

CHAPTER  5  
The  First  Threshold  
February  2023  —  Lucknow  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
February  9,  2023  |  Library  |  11:07  AM  
The  notification  arrived.  The  one  I  had  been  waiting  for  since  the  morning.  The  SSC  
website
 
slowly
 
loaded
 
on
 
my
 
screen:
 
'Combined
 
Graduate
 
Level
 
Examination
 
(Tier-I),
 
2022
 
–
 
Declaration
 
of
 
Result.'
 
My  hand  trembled  slightly  over  the  mouse.  Results  in  this  world  didn't  come  in  neatly  
packaged
 
scorecards;
 
they
 
came
 
in
 
massive,
 
unforgiving
 
PDFs.
 
I
 
downloaded
 
'List
 
3'
 
and
 
hit
 
Ctrl+F.
 
I
 
typed
 
my
 
10-digit
 
roll
 
number.
 
Enter.
 
The  page  buffered.  One  second.  Two  seconds.  
An  orange  highlight  flashed  across  the  screen.  My  roll  number.  My  name.  The  UR  
cutoff
 
was
 
114.27,
 
and
 
I
 
was
 
safely
 
above
 
it.
 
I
 
had
 
qualified.
 
There  was  no  sudden  jump  of  joy.  Nobody  screamed.  Inside  my  chest,  something  
simply
 
settled—like
 
a
 
tight
 
rope
 
that
 
had
 
been
 
stretched
 
for
 
months
 
finally
 
loosening
 
by
 
a
 
fraction
 
of
 
an
 
inch.
 
That
 
was
 
it.
 
That
 
was
 
all
 
it
 
was.
 
I  texted  Bhaiya:  'Qualify  ho  gaya.'  Three  minutes  later,  his  reply  came:  'Achha.  Padhai  
jaari
 
rakho.'
 
Mummy
 
called
 
five
 
minutes
 
after
 
that.
 
She
 
asked
 
the
 
same
 
things
 
she
 
always
 
asked—had
 
I
 
eaten?
 
Was
 
I
 
sleeping
 
properly?
 
And
 
then,
 
slipped
 
somewhere

"Beta,
 
achha
 
kiya."
 
In
 
the

I
 
heard
 
Papa’s

say,
 
"Shabash."
 
It
 
wasn't
 
loud,
 
but
 
it
 
was
 
there.
 
This  was  just  the  screening.  Nearly  three  and  a  half  lakh  students  had  qualified  for  
Tier-1.
 
The
 
real
 
game—Tier-2—was
 
about
 
to
 
begin,
 
and
 
the
 
competition
 
would
 
be
 
brutal.
 
I  put  my  phone  face  down.  Locked  the  screen.  Pulled  out  my  books  and  picked  up  my  
pen.
 
This
 
was
 
what
 
I
 
had
 
to
 
do.
 
Only
 
this.
 
The  library  had  a  different  texture  on  result  days.  The  victories  were  muted,  and  the  
grief
 
was
 
suffocating.
 
Sivi  sat  at  Seat  18.  She  had  checked  her  result  too;  she  had  cleared  it.  She  closed  her  
notebook
 
and
 
stared
 
blankly
 
out
 
the
 
small
 
basement
 
window.
 
She
 
didn't
 
say
 
a
 
word,
 
and
 
neither
 
did
 
I.
 
What
 
could
 
I
 
say?
 
This
 
was
 
her
 
first
 
attempt.
 
She
 
was
 
from
 
a
 
Biology
 
background,
 
perpetually
 
terrified
 
of
 
Math,
 
yet
 
she
 
had
 
cracked
 
Tier-1.
 
But

the
 
massive
 
wall
 
of
 
Tier-2
 
Mathematics
 
stood
 
right
 
in
 
front
 
of
 
her.
 
No
 
one
 
needed
 
to
 
explain

to
 
her;
 
she
 
knew.
 
And
 
knowing
 
is
 
always
 
the
 
heaviest
 
part.
 
Yadav’s  text  arrived  in  the  evening.  Just  three  words:  'Bhai.  Nahi  hua.'  
I  replied:  'Kal  baat  karte  hain.'  
He  sent  a  thumbs-up  emoji.  That  was  it.  
I  met  him  at  the  tapri  that  night.  He  was  already  sitting  there,  a  cup  of  tea  in  his  hand,  
wearing
 
the
 
specific
 
expression
 
of
 
a
 
man
 
who
 
isn't
 
dramatically
 
disappointed,
 
just
 
deeply
 
tired.
 
"Naam  nahi  mila  PDF  mein,"  he  said.  "2-3  number  se  reh  gaya  shayad  —  marks  
aayenge
 
tab
 
pata
 
chalega."
 
I  took  a  sip  of  my  tea.  "Seema  ko  bataya?"  
For  the  first  time  that  night,  a  small,  genuine  smile  touched  Yadav's  lips.  "Haan  bhai.  
Use
 
in
 
sab
 
SSC
 
vagaira
 
se
 
matlab
 
toh
 
hai
 
nahi
 
—
 
woh
 
apna
 
UPPCS
 
mein
 
lagi
 
rehti
 
hai.
 
Par
 
usne

chai
 
pe
 
pooch
 
liya

ka.
 
Maine
 
bola
 
fail
 
ho
 
gaya.
 
Woh
 
thodi
 
der
 
shant
 
rahi.
 
Phir
 
boli
 
—
 
'tum
 
karte
 
rehna.'"
 
I  looked  at  him.  
"Bhai,  paper  fail  hua,"  Yadav  continued  softly.  "Par  woh  UPPCS  wali  hoke  bhi  boli  —  
karte
 
rehna.
 
Seedha
 
mujhe
 
bola.
 
Yeh
 
chhota
 
nahi
 
hai
 
yaar."
 
It  wasn't.  It  wasn't  small  at  all.  
Yadav  pulled  out  a  cigarette  but  didn't  light  it.  He  just  rolled  it  between  his  fingers,  an  
old
 
habit.
 
"Agli
 
baar
 
karunga.
 
Abhi
 
Seema
 
ka
 
PCS
 
ka
 
notification
 
aane
 
wala
 
hai,
 
uski
 
thodi
 
help
 
kar
 
dunga
 
notes
 
vagaira
 
mein.
 
Toh
 
matlab
 
hai
 
abhi
 
ka."
 
I  didn't  say  anything.  But  somewhere  inside,  I  recognized  it—the  profound  quiet  that  
comes
 
when
 
a
 
person
 
finds
 
something
 
larger
 
than
 
their
 
own
 
pain
 
to
 
hold
 
onto.
 
Not  everyone  fought  the  exam.  Some  people,  like  Verma  at  Seat  42,  fought  
themselves.
 
He
 
was
 
a
 
UPSC
 
aspirant
 
who
 
had
 
joined
 
in
 
the
 
second
 
week
 
of
 
February.
 
Tired
 
eyes,
 
laptop
 
bag.
 
He
 
was
 
fighting
 
for
 
the

of
 
an
 
ex-girlfriend,
 
Richa,
 
who
 
had
 
already
 
married
 
someone
 
else.
 
Whenever
 
he
 
was
 
alone,
 
he
 
desperately
 
tried
 
to
 
talk
 
to
 
someone,
 
anyone,
 
to
 
fill
 
the
 
void.
 
He
 
was
 
trapped
 
in
 
a
 
loop,
 
and
 
the
 
library
 
was
 
just
 
the
 
arena
 
he
 
had
 
chosen
 
to
 
bleed
 
in.
 
 A  Few  Days  Later  |  Sunday  Morning  
It  was  Sunday,  but  I  had  a  mock  test  pending.  I  left  my  rented  room,  taking  the  
shortcut
 
through
 
the
 
narrow
 
alley
 
toward
 
the
 
library.
 
The
 
February
 
sun
 
was
 
mild—the
 
kind
 
of
 
sunlight
 
that
 
doesn't
 
push
 
the
 
winter
 
away,
 
but
 
gently
 
warns
 
you
 
that
 
it's
 
leaving.
 
About  fifty  meters  down  the  alley,  I  saw  someone  walking  toward  me.  It  was  Naina.  
But  she  looked  entirely  different.  Oversized  grey  t-shirt  hanging  loosely  off  her  
shoulders.
 
Black
 
shorts
 
ending
 
above
 
her
 
knees.
 
Her
 
hair
 
was
 
tied
 
in
 
a
 
messy,
 
hurried
 
bun.
 
There
 
was
 
absolutely
 
nothing
 
on
 
her
 
face—no
 
makeup,
 
no
 
armor.
 
It
 
was
 
the
 
first
 
time
 
I
 
was
 
seeing
 
her
 
without
 
her
 
deliberate
 
preparation.
 
In  her  hand  was  a  leash.  At  the  end  of  it  trotted  a  brown-and-white  spotted  dog,  its  tail  
wagging
 
furiously.
 
I  was  walking  quietly,  hoping  to  pass  by,  when  her  eyes  found  me.  
"Hey!  Library!"  she  called  out  from  a  distance.  
I  stopped.  She  walked  up  to  me,  a  faint  smile  on  her  face.  "Sunday  bhi?"  
"Haan.  Mock  test  hai."  
"Dedication."  
The  dog  trotted  up  to  me  and  began  sniffing  my  shoes.  I  instinctively  took  a  half-step  
back.
 
"Dogs  pasand  nahi?"  she  asked.  
"Khaas  nahi.  Comfortable  nahi  hoon."  
"Yeh  Zoi  hai.  Female.  Friendly.  Zoi,  sit."  
The  dog  sat  immediately.  Naina  ran  her  hand  over  Zoi's  head—a  natural,  incredibly  
gentle
 
gesture.
 
There
 
was
 
no
 
performance
 
in
 
it.
 
"Touch
 
kar
 
sakte
 
ho.
 
Kaategi
 
nahi."
 
I  extended  my  hand,  hesitant.  The  fur  on  Zoi's  head  was  soft  and  warm.  Her  tail  
started
 
wagging
 
again,
 
sweeping
 
against
 
the
 
dust
 
on
 
the
 
road.
 
"Dekha?  She  likes  you,"  Naina  said.  
"Tum  yahan  rehti  ho?"  I  asked.  
She  pointed  toward  a  building  at  the  end  of  the  lane.  "Woh  hai  mera  ghar.  White  gate  
wala."
 
I  looked.  It  was  an  independent  villa.  Pristine  white  walls.  An  intercom  at  the  gate.  A  
sleek
 
black
 
BMW
 
parked
 
in
 
the
 
porch,
 
right
 
next
 
to
 
her
 
white
 
i20.
 
My
 
brain
 
registered
 
the
 
details
 
automatically.
 
Garment
 
business.
 
Villa.
 
BMW.
 
Mahanagar.
 
And
 
here
 
I
 
was,
 
living
 
in
 
a
 
rented
 
room
 
with
 
my
 
brother,
 
entirely
 
dependent
 
on
 
him
 
for
 
my
 
library
 
fees.
 
We  were  from  two  entirely  different  realities.  It  was  a  simple  fact.  There  was  no  pain  in  
the
 
observation,
 
no
 
dramatic
 
conclusion
 
to
 
be
 
drawn.
 
It
 
was
 
just
 
a
 
note
 
filed
 
away
 
in
 
my
 
head.
 
"Papa  ka  hai,"  she  said.  Her  voice  was  flat,  as  if  she  were  talking  about  a  stranger's  
property.
 
"Waise
 
meri
 
unse
 
zyada
 
baat
 
hoti
 
nahi
 
hai."
 
"Achha."  
She  paused.  She  shifted  the  leash  to  her  other  hand  and  looked  at  me.  "Waise,  main  
Naina."
 
It  was  our  first  proper  introduction.  Since  December,  we  had  only  recognized  each  
other
 
as
 
faces
 
at
 
a
 
tea
 
stall.
 
"Eklavya,"  I  said.  
She  took  a  second,  repeating  the  name  in  her  head.  I  could  see  the  gears  turning  
behind
 
her
 
eyes.
 
"Eklavya.
 
Okay.
 
Anyway,
 
main
 
chalti
 
hoon.
 
Zoi
 
ko
 
aur
 
ghumana
 
hai."
 
"Haan.  All  the  best."  
"Thanks.  Tum  bhi."  
She  walked  away,  Zoi  trotting  happily  beside  her.  I  turned  back  toward  the  library.  
I  had  seen  her  in  her  natural  element  for  the  first  time.  The  messy  bun  in  the  morning  
sun.
 
No
 
defenses.
 
That
 
Naina
 
was
 
different
 
from
 
the
 
untouchable
 
girl
 
at
 
the
 
coaching
 
center—or
 
maybe
 
she
 
was
 
exactly
 
the
 
same.
 
Maybe
 
this
 
was
 
the
 
version
 
she
 
kept
 
only
 
for
 
herself.
 
We  met  at  the  library  gate.  We  drank  tea  at  the  tapri.  We  belonged  to  two  completely  
different
 
worlds,
 
but
 
in
 
those
 
brief
 
intersections,
 
we
 
occupied
 
the
 
same
 
space.
 
And
 
for
 
now,
 
maybe
 
that
 
was
 
enough.

CHAPTER  6  Splintered  Worlds  February  –  March  2023  —  Lucknow  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
February  27,  2023  |  8:30  PM  |  The  Library  
I  was  staring  at  a  sheet  of  Statistics  formulas.  It  was  my  last  revision.  The  SSC  CGL  
Tier-2
 
exam
 
was
 
four
 
days
 
away.
 
My  phone  vibrated.  It  was  Bhaiya.  
"Bhai...  Pihu  ko  phir  se  fever  aaya  hai.  Saans  phool  rahi  hai.  Hospital  ja  rahe  hain."  
My  heart  stopped.  "Main  aa  raha  hoon."  
"Nahi  bhai.  Tu  library  mein  padh.  Hum  manage  kar  lenge."  
"Nahi.  Main  aa  raha  hoon.  10  minute  mein."  
I  cut  the  call  and  packed  my  bag.  It  was  8:45  PM.  I  caught  an  auto  to  Mahanagar.  It  
took
 
fifteen
 
minutes.
 
When  I  reached  the  flat,  they  were  just  leaving.  Pihu  was  in  Bhabhi’s  arms,  wrapped  
tightly
 
in
 
a
 
blanket.
 
Just
 
a
 
tiny

Her

was

fast,
 
a

"Chalo.  Meri  bike  pe.  Auto  mein  time  lagega,"  I  said.  
Bhabhi  sat  on  the  back  with  Pihu  pressed  against  her  chest.  Bhaiya  sat  behind  me.  
We
 
tore
 
through
 
the
 
night
 
streets.
 
Fatima  Hospital  |  Emergency  |  9:15  PM  
The  doctor  checked  her  immediately.  Stethoscope.  Temperature.  He  clipped  a  pulse  
oximeter
 
onto
 
Pihu’s
 
tiny
 
finger.
 
The
 
glowing
 
red
 
numbers
 
on
 
the
 
screen
 
stabilized
 
at
 
88%.
 
"Pneumonia  hai.  Oxygen  level  critical  hai  —  95  se  upar  hona  chahiye,"  the  doctor

his

"Admit
 
karna

ICU
 
mein

Bhabhi’s  hands  were  shaking  violently.  Bhaiya  silently  filled  out  the

I  stepped  aside  and  called  Papa  in  Kusumpur.  "Papa,  Pihu  hospital  mein  admit  hai.  
Pneumonia.
 
Kal
 
subah
 
aa
 
jaao."
 
Papa’s  voice  was  steady,  but  I  could  hear  the  age  in  it.  "Theek  hai  beta.  Pehli  bus  se

Pihu  cried.  It  wasn't  a  scream;  she  didn't  know  how  to  scream  yet.  It  was  a  weak,

Bhabhi

March  20,  2023  |  6:45  PM  |  The  Park

"Dimaag  slow  ho  jaata  hai.  Cheezein  settle  hoti  hain."  
"Hm."  She  said  the  word  as  if  she  were  filing  the  information  away.

She  turned  to  me  again.  "Woh  exam  —  hua  kuch?"  
The  stillness  of  that  number—280—was  sitting  quietly  in  my  chest.  
"Possibility  hai,"  I  said  softly.  
Naina  looked  at  me.  No  ego,  no  sarcasm.  Just  a  straight,  unshielded  look.  It  was  a  
look
 
she
 
rarely
 
gave
 
anyone.
 
"Nice,"  she  said  slowly.  "You  look  like  someone  jise  pata  hai  ki  uska  ho  jaayega."  
"Shayad."  
It  was  completely  dark  now.  We  both  stood  up  at  the  same  time  and  turned  in  
opposite
 
directions.
 
She
 
took
 
two
 
steps,
 
then
 
stopped.
 
Without
 
turning
 
around,
 
she
 
asked:
 
"Woh  Harari  wali  book  —  naam  kya  hai?"  
"Sapiens."  
"Hm."  
She  walked  away.  I  turned  toward  the  library.  
Sapiens.  She  had  memorized  it.  Or  maybe  she  already  knew  it  and  was  just  
confirming.
 
It
 
amounted
 
to
 
the
 
same
 
thing.
 
 
March  20,  2023  |  9:30  PM  |  The  Flat  
I  lay  on  my  stomach  on  the  floor  of  my  room.  
I  could  hear  Pihu’s  faint  noises  from  the  living  room.  She  was  doing  better.  They  had  
brought
 
her
 
home
 
from
 
the

three

She
 
still

oxygen
 
support
 
occasionally

she
 
was
 
here.
 
She
 
woke
 
up
 
in
 
the
 
morning.
 
She
 
reached
 
out
 
her
 
tiny
 
hand.
 
That  was  enough.  For  now,  it  was  enough.  
I  closed  my  eyes.  My  mind  drifted  back  to  the  park.  The  dim  light.  Naina.  
She  must  have  walked  back  down  the  alley  by  now.  Through  the  white  gate.  Past  the  
BMW.
 
Into
 
the
 
lit-up
 
porch.
 
And  I  was  here.  Five  hundred  meters  away.  Three  floors  up  in  a  rented  flat.  
Five  hundred  meters.  A  six-minute  walk.  
But  those  five  hundred  meters  weren't  something  you  could  measure  with  footsteps.  
They
 
were
 
the
 
distance
 
between
 
morning
 
tea
 
at
 
a
 
tapri
 
and
 
filter

The
 
distance
 
between

and
 
preparing
 
for
 
IIMs.
 
The
 
distance
 
between
 
a
 
1300-rupee
 
library
 
fee
 
and
 
a
 
BMW.
 
Two  different  worlds.  Two  different  destinies.  In  the  same  city.  On  the  same  evening.  
Sitting
 
on
 
the
 
same
 
bench.
 
Sometimes,  distance  isn't  measured  in  meters.  It's  measured  in  centuries.  
I  stared  at  the  ceiling.  
There  was  one  thing  I  couldn't  logically  process,  no  matter  how  hard  I  tried.  I  read  
Harari.
 
I
 
read
 
Krishnamurti.
 
I
 
was
 
a
 
practiced

I
 
watched
 
people—at
 
Seat
 
17,
 
at
 
the
 
tapri,
 
Rajesh

Yadav,
 
Sivi—but
 
I
 
always
 
maintained
 
a
 
distance.
 
I
 
never
 
stepped
 
into
 
their
 
mess.
 
With  Naina,  that  distance  had  collapsed.  
She  had  been  different  from  day  one.  In  December,  when  she  asked  for  the  
lighter—the
 
two
 
steps
 
forward,
 
the
 
smoke
 
in
 
my
 
face,
 
that
 
"makes
 
sense

Even
 
then,
 
there
 
was
 
a
 
spark.
 
But
 
I
 
had
 
rationalized
 
it
 
away.
 
An
 
interesting
 
encounter.
 
Nothing
 
more.
 
In
 
February,
 
seeing
 
her
 
in
 
the
 
sun
 
with
 
a
 
messy
 
bun
 
and
 
a
 
dog.
 
Another
 
interesting
 
encounter.
 
But  today?  
Today  defied  explanation.  
She  hadn't  broken  down  completely.  She  didn't  cry.  She  didn't  suddenly  become  easy  
to
 
read.
 
She
 
remained
 
exactly
 
who
 
she
 
was—controlled,
 
sharp,
 
hidden
 
behind
 
her
 
wall.
 
But

one

of

that

had

And
 
I
 
was
 
the
 
witness
 
to

"You  look  like  someone  jise  pata  hai  ki  uska  ho  jaayega."  
She  had  said  it  with  a  straight  gaze.  Without  sarcasm.  And  there  was  something  in  
that
 
look—recognition.
 
She
 
had
 
seen
 
something
 
in
 
me
 
that
 
she
 
understood,
 
something
 
she
 
respected.
 
I  pressed  the  heels  of  my  hands  into  my  eyes.  
My  mind  was  in  control.  It  always  was.  Today  was  just  a  park  bench,  a  conversation,  a  
strange
 
evening.
 
Tomorrow
 
morning,
 
the
 
routine
 
would
 
resume.
 
The
 
5:30
 
alarm.
 
The
 
6:15
 
tea.
 
The
 
library.
 
Seat
 
17.
 
The
 
final
 
result
 
could
 
drop
 
any
 
day.
 
That  was  more  important.  That  was  always  going  to  be  more  important.  
But  that  one  second—when  her  wall  trembled—refused  to  fade.  
Some  memories  don't  ask  for  permission  before  they  move  in.  
I  heard  Bhabhi’s  voice  from  the  living  room,  singing  a  soft,  low  lullaby  to  Pihu.  I  
closed
 
my
 
eyes
 
and
 
let
 
the
 
sleep
 
take
 
over.

CHAPTER  7  The  Sentinel  March  –  April  2023  —  Lucknow  /  Kusumpur  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
A.  The  Weight  of  a  Smile  March  30,  2023  |  Morning  |  Bhaiya’s  Flat  
Every  morning,  the  first  thing  Pihu  did  when  she  woke  up  was  wave  her  hand  from  
where
 
she
 
lay.
 
It
 
wasn't
 
accompanied
 
by
 
a
 
sound
 
or
 
a
 
demand.
 
It
 
was
 
just
 
a
 
tiny
 
hand
 
reaching
 
upward,
 
as
 
if
 
she
 
were
 
checking
 
to
 
see
 
if
 
the
 
world
 
was
 
still
 
there.
 
When  leaving  for  the  library,  I  would  bend  down  and  extend  my  finger.  She  would  grab  
it.
 
That
 
was
 
our
 
wordless
 
morning
 
ritual.
 
But  today,  she  didn't  just  grab  it.  She  held  onto  it  for  a  moment  longer  than  usual,  and  
then
 
gave
 
a
 
soft
 
tug,
 
pulling
 
my
 
hand
 
toward
 
her.
 
It
 
was
 
as
 
if
 
she
 
were
 
saying,
 
"Aaj
 
kuch
 
aur."
 
I  understood.  I  picked  her  up.  
It  was  the  first  time  I  was  holding  her  properly  since  she  came  back  from  the  hospital.  
She
 
was
 
incredibly
 
light—so
 
light
 
that
 
for
 
a
 
split
 
second,
 
a
 
spike
 
of
 
fear
 
hit
 
my
 
chest.
 
But
 
her
 
grip
 
on
 
my
 
shirt
 
was
 
tight.
 
She
 
knew
 
exactly
 
how
 
to
 
hold
 
on.
 
I  brought  her  close  to  my  face.  Her  round,  medium-sized  eyes  stared  back  at  me  from  
inches
 
away.
 
No
 
filter.
 
No
 
questions.
 
Just
 
looking.
 
I  gently  tapped  my  finger  on  her  nose.  She  scrunched  it.  
And  then,  she  smiled.  
It  was  her  first  real  smile.  It  was  so  small  that  if  I  hadn't  been  looking  closely,  I  would  
have
 
missed
 
it.
 
But
 
it
 
was
 
there.
 
The
 
corners
 
of
 
her
 
lips
 
tilted
 
upward,
 
and
 
her
 
eyes
 
grew
 
a
 
little
 
rounder.
 
I  tapped  her  nose  again.  She  smiled  wider  this  time,  as  if  confirming,  "Haan,  yeh  wali  
cheez
 
mujhe
 
pasand
 
hai."
 
Bhabhi  walked  out  of  the  kitchen  and  stopped.  She  covered  her  mouth  with  her  hand.  
Her
 
eyes
 
filled
 
with
 
tears
 
for
 
a
 
second
 
before
 
she
 
blinked
 
them
 
away
 
and
 
hurried
 
back
 
to
 
her
 
tea
 
preparations.
 
Bhaiya  walked  into  the  living  room,  his  IT  company  ID  card  still  hanging  around  his  
neck.
 
He
 
saw
 
us,
 
quietly
 
took
 
off
 
the
 
ID,
 
and
 
walked
 
over.
 
"Kab  se?"  he  asked,  looking  at  me.  
"Abhi.  Do  minute  pehle."  
He  didn't  say  anything  else.  He  stepped  closer  and  gently  stroked  Pihu’s  head.  Pihu  
looked
 
at
 
him,
 
then
 
at
 
me,
 
then
 
back
 
at
 
him.
 
She
 
seemed
 
to
 
decide
 
that
 
both
 
of
 
us
 
were
 
acceptable.
 
Bhaiya  gripped  my  shoulder.  Just  for  a  second.  He  didn't  speak.  He  just  turned  and  
walked
 
to
 
his
 
room.
 
I  stood  there,  holding  Pihu.  She  was  smiling.  For  now,  this  was  all  there  was.  And  it  
was
 
more
 
than
 
enough.
 
 
A  Week  Later  |  Kusumpur  
A  bus  from  Lucknow.  Three  hours.  
Kusumpur  was  just  four  kilometers  away  from  Ayodhya,  but  a  three-hour  journey  
doesn't
 
just
 
mean
 
three
 
hours
 
when
 
you
 
are
 
returning
 
home
 
after
 
months.
 
It
 
means
 
the
 
specific
 
dust
 
of
 
that
 
road,
 
the
 
shape
 
of
 
those
 
trees,
 
the
 
smell
 
of
 
the
 
earth
 
that
 
simply
 
doesn't
 
exist
 
in
 
the
 
city.
 
Papa  was  standing  at  the  gate  when  I  arrived.  He  didn't  say  anything.  His  expression  
just
 
said,
 
Come
 
inside.
 
Mummy  called  out  from  the  kitchen  immediately,  "Haath  dho  le,  khana  lag  raha  hai."  
Everything  was  exactly  the  same.  It  always  would  be.  
That  night,  after  dinner,  Papa  asked  the  only  question  that  mattered.  "Result  kab  
aayega?"
 
"Pata  nahi.  Kuch  mahine."  
"Padhta  reh,"  he  said.  Just  that.  
From  the  other  room,  Mummy  yelled,  "Neend  aa  rahi  hai  use,  chhod  do  ab."  
I  went  to  bed.  The  silence  of  the  village  was  entirely  different  from  the  silence  of  the  
library.
 
It
 
was
 
deeper,
 
older.
 
It
 
felt
 
as
 
if
 
the
 
ground
 
itself
 
was
 
breathing
 
beneath
 
me.
 
 
B.  Shared  Geography  April  2023  |  Library  Canteen  
The  routine  had  returned.  The  5:30  AM  alarm.  The  6:15  tea.  The  basement.  Seat  17.  
I  was  standing  in  the  canteen  line  behind  Sivi.  Even  during  the  break,  her  notebook  
was
 
open,
 
her
 
pen
 
moving
 
across
 
the
 
page.
 
"Kitne  bane?"  I  asked  from  behind  her.  
She  looked  up.  "255  ke  aaspaas.  Rough.  Normalisation  ke  baad  pata  nahi."  
I  knew  exactly  where  that  number  stood  in  the  brutal  hierarchy  of  SSC  cutoffs,  but  I  
didn't
 
say
 
anything.
 
"Tum?"  she  asked.  
"280.  Rough."  
She  stopped  writing.  She  looked  at  me  for  a  second.  "Comfortable  zone  mein  ho."  
"Shayad."  
We  both  got  our  tea.  The  canteen  was  filled  with  its  usual  cacophony—the  scraping  of  
plastic
 
chairs,
 
the
 
hiss
 
of
 
boiling
 
water,
 
the
 
low
 
hum
 
of
 
anxiety.
 
"Tum  kahan  se  ho?"  she  asked  casually.  It  wasn't  idle  curiosity;  she  genuinely  wanted  
to
 
know.
 
"Kusumpur.  Faizabad  ke  paas,"  I  replied.  
She  turned  fully  toward  me.  "Faizabad?  Matlab  Ayodhya?"  
"Haan.  Ab  toh  sab  Ayodhya  hi  bol  rahe  hain."  
"Main  bhi  Ayodhya  se  hoon,"  she  said,  sounding  slightly  surprised  herself.  "Civil  
Lines.
 
Tum?"
 
"Bahar  ka  gaon  hai.  Char  kilometer."  
She  smiled—a  genuine,  unguarded  smile.  "Itne  mahine  ho  gaye,  pehli  baar  koi  mila  jo  
samjhe
 
ki
 
Faizabad
 
aur
 
Ayodhya
 
ek
 
hi
 
jagah
 
hai."
 
"Lucknow  mein  yeh  explain  karna  padta  hai,"  I  said.  
"Haan."  She  paused,  staring  into  her  tea.  "Wahan  ki  Ram  ki  Paidi  yaad  aati  hai  kabhi  
kabhi.
 
Subah
 
wali
 
smell
 
—
 
woh
 
dhoop,
 
woh
 
paani.
 
Hostel
 
mein
 
raat
 
ko."
 
I  took  a  sip  of  my  tea.  "Aati  hai."  
"Tum  kitne  saal  se  bahar  ho?"  
"June  se.  9-10  mahine."  
"Pehla  saal  sabse  ajeeb  hota  hai,"  she  said  softly.  "Ghar  ki  har  cheez  miss  karti  thi  —  
par
 
wapas
 
jaana
 
bhi
 
nahi
 
chahti
 
thi.
 
Jaise
 
dono
 
jagah
 
adhoori
 
hoon."
 
I  didn't  reply.  That  feeling  was  exact.  The  city  slowly  claims  pieces  of  you,  but  the  
village
 
never
 
entirely
 
lets
 
you
 
go.
 
You
 
just
 
end
 
up
 
existing
 
somewhere
 
in
 
the
 
middle.
 
"Papa  ne  kuch  kaha  result  ke  baare  mein?"  she  asked.  
"Padhta  reh.  Bas  itna."  
She  let  out  a  short,  knowing  laugh.  "Mere  papa  ne  bola  tha  —  'beta,  wahan  bhi  log  
hain
 
jo
 
maths
 
jaante
 
hain.'
 
Background
 
check
 
tha
 
woh
 
—
 
chhupa
 
hua."
 
"Tumhara  maths  weak  hai?"  
"Biology  background  hai.  Maths  baad  mein  seekha  —  khud  se,  raat  ko,  hostel  mein.  
Kisi
 
se
 
help
 
nahi
 
maangi
 
kyunki
 
—
 
I
 
don't
 
know.
 
Pride?
 
Ya
 
shayad
 
darr
 
tha
 
ki
 
koi
 
bola
 
ki
 
yeh
 
tumhare
 
bas
 
ka
 
nahi."
 
"Par  kiya."  
"Karna  pada."  She  paused.  "255  mein  se  kitna  maths  ka  hai?"  
"Kaafi."  
"Pata  hai."  She  finished  her  tea  and  picked  up  her  notebook.  "Agli  baar  karungi.  Ab  
pata
 
hai
 
kahan
 
khatam
 
hoti
 
hai
 
preparation."
 
She  walked  back  to  her  seat.  I  walked  back  to  Seat  17.  Two  people  from  the  same  city,  
arriving
 
here
 
through
 
entirely
 
different
 
roads.
 
It
 
was
 
a
 
shared
 
geography
 
that
 
didn't
 
need
 
to
 
be
 
explained;
 
it
 
just
 
existed.
 
 
C.  The  List  of  Fictions  April  2023  |  Library  Parking  Lot  
I  walked  out  of  the  basement  into  the  blinding  afternoon  sun.  
Naina  was  in  the  parking  lot,  standing  by  her  white  i20.  She  had  mock  test  sheets  in  
one
 
hand
 
and
 
a
 
bag
 
slung
 
over
 
her
 
shoulder.
 
She
 
was
 
rummaging
 
through
 
the
 
bag,
 
probably
 
looking
 
for
 
her
 
keys.
 
She  saw  me.  For  a  fraction  of  a  second,  the  hesitation  from  that  night  in  the  park  
flashed
 
in
 
her
 
eyes.
 
But
 
she
 
recovered
 
instantly,
 
offering
 
a
 
curt
 
nod.
 
I
 
stopped
 
walking.
 
I
 
didn't
 
know
 
why.
 
"Aaj  coaching  thi?"  I  asked.  Neither  of  us  mentioned  that  night  on  the  bench.  It  was  
an
 
unspoken,
 
mutual
 
treaty.
 
"QA  session.  Boring  tha.  Sab  kuch  already  covered  tha,"  she  replied,  still  digging  
through
 
her
 
bag.
 
"Tum
 
itne
 
ghante
 
andar
 
kaise
 
rehte
 
ho?
 
Basement
 
mein.
 
Peeli
 
light.
 
Koi
 
khidki
 
nahi."
 
"Habit  ho  gayi."  
"Mujhe  claustrophobia  ho  jaata."

H.  A  Sweet  Fear  11:45  PM  |  The  Flat  
I  lay  on  my  stomach,  staring  at  the  ceiling.  
I  wasn't  replaying  the  conversation  in  my  head;  I  was  replaying  the  feeling.  There  was  
a
 
strange,
 
pervasive
 
calmness
 
inside
 
me,
 
as
 
if
 
someone
 
had
 
quietly

a

door
 
without

But  alongside  the  calm,  an  alarm  was  ringing.  
Attraction  isn't  dangerous.  You  can  ignore  attraction  by  setting  a  study  timer  and  
opening
 
a

But

It  was  a  fear.  A  very  sweet  fear.  
My  phone  vibrated  on  the  mattress.  The  screen  lit  up  the  dark  room.  
Naina  Sethi:  Reached  home?  
Simple.  No  possessiveness.  No  elaborate  paragraph.  Just  marking  her  presence.  
I  typed:  Haan.

A.  The  Waiting  Mode  April  28,  2023  |  Library  Basement  |  11:32  AM  
The  library  basement  was  as  cold  as  ever.  It  was  the  kind  of  cold  that  didn't  come  
from
 
the

conditioning,
 
but

The

Seat
 
17.
 
I  put  my  bag  down,  opened  my  notebook,  uncapped  my  pen,  capped  it  again,  and  set  
it
 
aside.
 
The  routine  had  returned—or  maybe  it  had  never  left.  It  had  just  been  temporarily

Upstairs  on  the  fourth  floor,  CAT  classes  would  be  running.  Lately,  I  had  started

My  phone  was  on  silent.  The  screen  was  dark.  11:32  AM.  I  had  a  question  half-solved  
in

of
 
me.
 
The  screen  lit  up.

I  looked  at  the  notification,  then  locked  the  screen  and  went  back  to  the  half-solved

left

The

was
 
short,
 
but
 
it
 
felt
 
complete.

The  final  result  was  still  pending.  Life  was  stuck  in  a  waiting  mode,  but  time  doesn't

B.  The  Quiet  Close  May  13  -  14  |  Result  Night  
The  result  dropped  at  11:45  PM.  Notifications  flooded  the  aspirant  groups.  I  put  my

aside
 
and

The  next  morning,  the  WhatsApp  forwards  were  everywhere.  Result  Out.  
I  went  to  the  library.  Opened  the  laptop.  Downloaded  the  massive  PDF.  The  loading  
bar
 
crawled
 
across
 
the

I  pressed  Ctrl+F  and  searched  for  my  name.  0/0  matches.  
I  typed  my  roll  number.  0/0  matches.  
I  stared  at  the  screen  for  exactly  one  second.  Then,  I  closed  the  laptop.  
No  sound  escaped  my  throat.  No  coherent  thought  formed  in  my  head.  It  felt  as  if

I  walked  straight  out  of  the  library  and  went  back  to  the  flat.  Bhabhi  wasn't  home.  The

was

"Nahi  hua,"  I  said.  
"Theek  hai  beta.  Padhai  mat  chhodna."  
I  cut  the  call.  Neither  of  us  had  another  sentence  in  us.  It  was  fine.

I  walked  out.  Mahanagar.  Then  Kapoorthala.  No  direction.  My  feet  knew  where  to  go,

C.  The  Unscripted  Page  May  14  |  6:00  PM  |  The  Flat  
A  knock  on  the  door.  I  opened  it.  
Naina.  
She  had  messaged  me  in  the  morning— "Ghar  pe  ho?"  I  had  replied,  "Haan,"  without  
thinking
 
much
 
about
 
it.
 
She  looked  at  my  face  for  a  second.  She  took  in  the  whole  picture—my  eyes,  my  
jawline,
 
that
 
heavy
 
stillness
 
that
 
usually
 
surrounded
 
me.
 
She
 
didn't
 
ask
 
a
 
single

She
 
just
 
walked
 
in.
 
She  looked  at  the  room—a  notebook  lying  open  on  the  floor,  two  empty  water  glasses,

half-drawn.
 
She
 
noticed
 
everything
 
but
 
said
 
nothing.
 
She
 
sat
 
on
 
the

her
 
back
 
straight.
 
It
 
was

always
 
operated—claiming
 
a

without

Silence  stretched  between  us.  The  fan  hummed  overhead.  An  auto  rattled  past  on  the  
street
 
outside.
 
"Result?"  she  finally  asked.  
"Hm."  
Nothing  else.  She  didn't  rush  to  fill  the  quiet.  A  minute  passed,  maybe  two.  The  room  
breathed
 
around
 
us.
 
"Chalo…  ek  result  se  life  decide  nahi  hoti—"  she  started,  then  stopped  herself.  "Yeh  
line
 
mujhe
 
bhi
 
fake
 
lag
 
रही
 
hai."
 
I  let  out  a  faint  laugh.  A  genuine  one,  directed  at  the  sheer  absurdity  of  the  situation.  It  
was
 
the
 
first
 
time
 
my
 
lips
 
had
 
curved
 
all
 
day.
 
She  leaned  forward  slightly,  looking  directly  into  my  eyes.  "Gussa  ho  ya  dukhi?"  
I  paused,  searching  for  the  right  word.  "Pata  nahi."  
She  nodded,  as  if  that  was  exactly  the  answer  she  had  expected.  
"Osho  ne  likha  tha  —  sadness  gives  depth,"  she  said.  A  small  beat.  "Aaj  tum  deep  lag  
rahe
 
ho.
 
Congratulations."
 
She  wasn't  trying  to  console  me.  She  wasn't  there  to  find  a  solution  to  my  pain  or  
offer
 
hollow
 
motivation.
 
She
 
was
 
just
 
there
.
 
There
 
is
 
a
 
massive
 
difference
 
between
 
the
 
two.
 
We  sat  there  for  another  hour.  Maybe  more.  Random  conversations—a  book,  a  stupid  
news
 
headline,
 
a
 
thought
 
that
 
appeared
 
from
 
nowhere
 
and
 
vanished
 
just
 
as
 
quickly.
 
And
 
in
 
between,
 
there
 
were
 
stretches
 
of
 
silence
 
that
 
didn't
 
feel
 
uncomfortable
 
at
 
all.
 
When  it  was  time  to  leave,  she  stopped  at  the  door.  She  rested  her  hand  on  the  
wooden
 
frame.
 
"Be.  Don't  try  to  become.  Aaj  ke  liye  enough  hai,"  she  said  softly.  
She  took  two  steps  out  into  the  hallway.  Then  she  stopped.  
She  turned  around,  walked  back  to  me—no  announcement,  no  dramatic  
build-up—and
 
kissed
 
me.
 
It  was  short.  Warm.  Entirely  devoid  of  cinematic  drama.  It  felt  as  if  this  had  been  
decided
 
a
 
long
 
time
 
ago,
 
and
 
neither
 
of
 
us
 
had
 
known
 
it.
 
When  she  pulled  back,  she  gave  me  that  specific,  naughty  smile.  The  one  that  clearly  
said
 
she
 
knew
 
exactly
 
what
 
she
 
had
 
just
 
done
 
to
 
my
 
head.
 
Then  she  walked  away.  
The  room  went  quiet  again.  I  stood  staring  at  the  empty  doorway  for  a  long  time.  But  
the
 
room
 
didn't
 
feel
 
empty
 
anymore.
 
It
 
had
 
been,
 
an
 
hour
 
ago.
 
Not
 
anymore.

A.  The  Smile  |  July  16  |  5:45  PM  
The  exam  was  tomorrow.  
I  needed  to  stop  overthinking  it,  so  I  got  out  of  the  room.  Bhabhi  pointed  toward  the  
pram.
 
"Pihu
 
ko
 
bhi
 
le
 
ja."
 
I
 
picked
 
her
 
up.
 
There
 
was
 
no

Just

usual

a

from
 
the

As  we  were  walking,  my  phone  vibrated  in  my  pocket.  Naina:  Best  of  luck  kal  ke  liye.  
I  looked  at  the  screen  and  typed  back:  Eklavya:  Haan.  Abhi  park  mein  hoon  Pihu  ke  
saath.
 
I  put  the  phone  back  in  my  pocket.  Two  minutes  later,  it  vibrated  again.  Naina:  Main  
bhi
 
kareeb
 
hoon.
 
Aa
 
sakti
 
hoon?
 
Eklavya:
 
Haan.
 
She  arrived  in  exactly  two  minutes.  Blue  jeans,  a  white  kurta,  one  earphone  dangling

She

into

She  stopped.  
Pihu  was  entirely  absorbed  in  her  own  fingers.  In,  out,  in,  out.  A  rhythm  that  belonged  
exclusively
 
to
 
her.
 
Naina  leaned  down,  then  second-guessed  herself  and  straightened  up.  Then  she  
leaned
 
down

I

couldn't

Finally,  she  made  her  own  decision.  She  squatted  beside  the  pram,  bringing  herself

to

She
 
didn't
 
look
 
at

"Hi."  
No  response.  Pihu’s  fingers  kept  moving.  
Naina  didn't  look  uncomfortable,  but  she  was  clearly  unsure  if  she  was  doing  it  right.  
She
 
glanced
 
up
 
at
 
me
 
for
 
a
 
fraction
 
of
 
a

I
 
just
 
gave

Yahi
 
theek
 
hai.
 
Bas

She  stood  up.  We  started  walking  together.  
At  one  point,  Naina  reached  out  a  hand  toward  the  pram's  handle,  then  pulled  it  back.  
A
 
few
 
steps
 
later,
 
she
 
reached
 
out

This

grabbed

sharing

with

She
 
didn't
 
say
 
a
 
word.
 
"Kitni  badi  hai?"  she  asked  quietly.  "Dedh  saal."  
"Haan."  She  didn't  ask  anything  else.  She  wasn't  trying  to  dissect  or  understand  the  
medical

she
 
was

being

There
 
is
 
a
 
massive
 
difference
 
between
 
the

We  kept  walking.  The  evening  slowly  began  to  fade.  
As  we  reached  the  gate  to  leave,  Naina  leaned  down  one  more  time.  She  had  gotten  
slightly
 
used
 
to
 
the
 
rhythm
 
by
 
now.
 
And  then  it  happened.  
A  mild  gust  of  wind  blew  past.  The  wheel  of  the  pram  hit  a  narrow  crack  in  the  
pavement
 
and
 
paused
 
for
 
a
 
second.
 
Pihu’s
 
fingers
 
stopped
 
moving.
 
She
 
looked
 
up
 
toward
 
the
 
sky,
 
but
 
Naina
 
was
 
right
 
in
 
her
 
line
 
of

And  without  any  signal,  without  any  logical  reason,  Pihu  smiled.  
It  was  that  smile.  The  tiny,  rare  one  that  barely  touched  the  corners  of  her  lips.  Naina  
didn't
 
know
 
how
 
rare
 
it
 
was.
 
But
 
I
 
did.
 
I  didn't  tell  her.  
Naina  stayed  silent  for  a  second,  mesmerized,  then  slowly  stood  up.  
"Kal  ka  paper,"  she  said  softly.  "Ho  jaayega."  "Theek  se  so  jaana."  
She  turned  and  walked  away  toward  her  world.

iON  Digital  Zone.  Seat  4C-12.  
The  start  was  good.  The  Reasoning  section  was  flowing  smoothly.  There  was  a

C.  Twenty-Eight  |  August  1  |  Night  
The  detailed  result  was  out.  
I  checked  the  breakdown.  Reasoning:  28.xx  
I  stopped  breathing.  
In  three  attempts,  and  countless  mock  tests,  I  had  never  scored  below  a  45  in  
Reasoning.
 
Even
 
in

A

The  doubt  that  had  been  gnawing  at  my  insides  for  the  last  three  weeks  was  no  longer

I  wasn't  afraid  of  losing.  But  losing  without  understanding  the  core  reason  why  you

I  picked  up  the  phone.  Naina’s  name  lit  up  the  screen.  
I  put  it  back  down.  
I  couldn't  tell  her.  This  burden  was  strictly  mine.  I  lay  back  and  stared  at  the  ceiling.  
 
D.  Graduate  Momos  |  A  Few  Days  Later  
There  was  a  new  board  at  Kapoorthala.  GRADUATE  MOMOS

CHAPTER  11  The  Clean  Memory  August  2023  —  Lucknow

A.  The  Demand  |  August  12  |  Afternoon  
My  phone  vibrated.  Naina:  Ananya  ki  party  hai  aaj  raat.  Renaissance.  Aa  jaao.  
Eklavya:  Nahi.  
Naina:  Eklavya.  
Eklavya:  Tumhari  friend  ki  party  hai.  Main  wahan  fit  nahi  honga.  
Naina:  Maine  nahi  poocha  fit  hoge  ya  nahi.  Maine  kaha  aa  jaao.  
A  pause.  
Eklavya:  Kaun  hoga  wahan?  
Naina:  Ananya.  Kabeer.  Kuch  aur  log.  Loud  music.  Drinks.  Tumhari  worst  nightmare.  
Eklavya:  Phir  kyun—  
Naina:  Kyunki  main  chahti  hoon.  
That  was  it.  I  knew  the  conversation  had  ended  right  there.  
 
B.  Renaissance  Sky  Bar  |  9:30  PM  
I  arrived  in  Naina’s  car.  
The  elevator  doors  opened,  and  the  noise  hit  me  before  anything  else—pulsing  bass,  
overlapping
 
laughter,
 
the
 
sharp
 
clinking
 
of
 
glasses.
 
I  stopped  for  a  fraction  of  a  second  before  stepping  out.  
Ananya  and  Kabeer  were  there,  along  with  a  few  others.  Names  were  introduced,  but  I  
knew
 
I
 
wouldn't
 
remember
 
them.
 
With
 
a
 
single
 
glance,
 
the
 
reality
 
was
 
obvious:
 
this
 
crowd
 
was
 
different.
 
Their
 
clothes
 
were
 
different.
 
They
 
moved

easy,

only
 
comes
 
when

like
 
this
 
are
 
a

of
 
your
 
existence.
 
I  looked  down  at  my  kurta.  It  was  clean.  It  was  pressed.  But  it  did  not  belong  here.  
No  one  was  looking  at  me,  but  I  was  looking  at  myself.  
I  grabbed  a  glass  from  the  bar—just  juice  and  ice.  I  needed  something  to  occupy  my  
hands.
 
The
 
chill
 
from
 
the
 
central
 
AC

artificial.
 
There

The

Everyone

On  the  dance  floor,  people  were  moving  to  the  beat  naturally,  as  if  they  hadn't  learned

but
 
were

Naina  met  Ananya.  An  easy  hug.  Laughter.  Words  exchanged  that  I  couldn't  hear  over  
the

That

was
 
entirely
 
hers.
 
I  stood  off  to  the  side.  I  couldn't  force  myself  to  go  deeper  inside,  and  I  couldn't  walk  
back
 
out
 
the
 
door.
 
Then,  I  looked  over  the  edge  of  the  terrace.  The  railing.  I  walked  toward  it.  
 
C.  The  River  
I  didn't  know  how  much  time  had  passed.  
The  deafening  noise  was  left  behind,  but  the  vibration  of  the  bass  still  travelled

Naina  walked  up.  She  had  come  straight  from  the  dance  floor.  She  was  slightly  out  of

She  looked  at  me,  and  then  she  looked  down  at  the  Gomti.  She  didn't  say  a  word.  
She  placed  her  hands  on  the  railing  and  stood  right  beside  me.  The  noise  was  still

D.  The  Car  |  11:57  PM  
The  driver  was  speeding.  The  roads  were  completely  empty,  the  yellow  streetlights  
blurring

The  car  went  over  a  speed  breaker,  lifting  slightly.  
Naina’s  shoulder  bumped  into  mine.  And  then,  her  hand  found  mine  and  gripped  it.  
This  time,  I  didn't  go  stiff.  I  didn't  pull  away.  I  just  let  my  hand  stay  exactly  where  it  
was.
 
For  two  minutes,  neither  of  us  spoke.  Then,  very  softly,  she  broke  the  silence.

"Accident  mein  gaya."  
The  speed  of  the  car  suddenly  felt  entirely  irrelevant.  
I  didn't  say  anything.  There  was  absolutely  nothing  to  say.  I  just  tightened  my  grip  on

E.  The  Drop-off  |  12:18  AM  
The  car  stopped  outside  my  building.  
She  paused  near  the  gate  and  looked  at  me—a  direct,  unshielded  look.  
I  didn't  think.  I  stepped  forward  and  pulled  her  into  a  hug.  
It  wasn't  tight.  It  was  just  steady.  
The  meaning  of  this  hug  was  entirely  different.  There  was  no  jealousy  in  it.  No  
ownership.
 
Not

It
 
was

Main
 
yahan
 
hoon.
 
She  froze  for  a  second.  Then,  her  arms  came  up,  and  she  held  onto  me.  
No  dramatic  dialogues.  Just  the  sound  of  breathing.  Her  heartbeat  was  steady  against  
my
 
chest.
 
Mine
 
was
 
slightly
 
fast.
 
Somewhere  in  the  distance  behind  us,  the  Gomti  kept  flowing.  
 
INTERLUDE  Naina's  Diary  |  August  12,  2023  |  Night  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
I  don’t  know  why  I  told  him  about  Rohan.  
I’ve  been  to  that  bar  maybe  fifteen  times.  Same  music.  Same  laughter.  Same  pointless  
conversations.
 
Tonight  was  different.  And  the  difference  wasn’t  the  bar.  It  was  him.  
He  stood  at  the  railing  for  God  knows  how  long.  Didn’t  drink.  Didn’t  dance.  Just  stood  
there
 
looking
 
at
 
the
 
river
 
like
 
it
 
owed
 
him
 
something.
 
Or
 
like
 
he
 
owed
 
it
 
something.
 
Main  wahan  gayi  —  shayad  check  karne.  Shayad  dekhne  ke  liye  ki  woh  kya  dekh  raha  
hai.
 
And  then  I  saw  it.  Woh  same  thi.  Wahi  stillness.  Wahi  beh-ti  hui  cheez.  
Most  people  look  at  lights.  He  was  looking  at  the  dark  between  them.  
He  wasn’t  uncomfortable  anymore.  He  was  just  there.  Present  in  a  way  most  people  
aren’t
 
even
 
at
 
places
 
they
 
actually
 
want
 
to
 
be.
 
Car  mein  —  speed  breaker  par  —  I  don’t  know  what  happened.  His  hand  was  just  
there.
 
And
 
then
 
Rohan
 
was
 
just
 
there.
 
Maine  Rohan  ka  naam  bahut  waqt  ke  baad  liya.  It  didn’t  feel  like  grief.  It  felt  like  
memory.
 
Clean
 
memory.
 
The
 
kind
 
you
 
can
 
hold
 
without
 
it
 
breaking
 
you.
 
Maine  Sentinel  ko  us  memory  mein  le  liya.  Bina  soche.  Woh  judge  nahi  karega  —  yeh  
pata
 
tha.
 
Par
 
yeh
 
nahi
 
pata
 
tha
 
ki
 
itna
 
easy
 
hoga.
 
Ghar  ke  neeche  woh  hug  —  I  was  not  expecting  that.  He  holds  himself  carefully.  
Deliberately.
 
But
 
tonight
 
he
 
just
 
moved.
 
No  calculation.  And  it  wasn’t  romantic.  Not  exactly.  It  was  something  more  basic  than  
that.
 
He  didn’t  fix  anything.  He  didn’t  promise  anything.  He  just  stayed.  
Mujhe  yeh  chahiye  tha  aaj.  Mujhe  pata  bhi  nahi  tha.  —  N.

The  Weight  of  Observation

A  quiet,  unspoken  pattern  had  established  itself  at  the  tapri.  
Her  classes  ended  at  11:00  AM.  I  would  be  waiting  outside  by  then.  We  occupied  the

Sometimes,  the  most  significant  shifts  are  the  ones  nobody  discusses.  They  just

One  morning,  she  shoved  her  phone  directly  in  front  of  my  face.  
"Yeh  dekho."  
It  was  her  Instagram  feed.  The  aesthetic  was  carefully  curated—muted  tones,

"Followers  kitne  hain?"  I  asked,  looking  at  the  screen.  
"Baarat  nahi  aa  rahi.  Focus  karo,"  she  snapped  playfully.  
"Okay.  Yeh  photo  ka  angle  forced  hai,"  I  said,  pointing  to  a  particularly  styled  shot.  
She  stopped.  "Forced  matlab?"  
"Yeh  tum  nahi  ho.  Yeh  tum  ho  jab  tum  soch  rahi  ho  ki  tumhe  kaisa  dikhna  chahiye."  
A  second  of  silence  fell  between  us.  
"Tum  critic  ho  ya  follower?"  
"Dono  nahi.  Bas  dekh  raha  tha."  
She  snatched  the  phone  back,  stared  at  the  screen  for  a  moment,  and  then  turned  it  
back
 
toward
 
me.
 
"Okay  fine.  Toh  kaunsi  real  lagti  hai?"  
I  scrolled  down.  I  stopped  at  a  photo  taken  inside  a  car.  The  sunlight  was  streaming  
through

"Yeh."

"Woh  accidentally  li  thi  Ananya  ne."

immediately
 
forced
 
her

back
 
into

unbothered

"Ab  tum  mujhe  sikhaoge  Instagram.  Tumhara  account  hai?"  
"Nahi."  
"Obviously."  She  took  a  sip  of  her  tea.  "Toh  tum  mujhe  social  media  advice  de  rahe  ho  
bina

She  started  laughing.  Properly  laughing.  The  kind  of  laughter  that  simply  cannot  be  
held
 
back.
 
 She  was  the  one  who  brought  up  the  philosophy  books.  
"Tumne  Osho  kyun  padha?  Seriously.  Woh  thoda...  cult-y  nahi  hai?"  
"Cult-y  log  bhi  kabhi  kabhi  sahi  baatein  bol  dete  hain,"  I  replied.  
"Example."  
"Woh  kehte  hain  —  tum  apni  feelings  ko  suppress  karte  karte  unke  ghulam  ban  jaate

She  stopped.  She  was  holding  her  paper  cup,  but  she  wasn't  drinking  from  it.  
"Aur  tum  personally  is  theory  ko  follow  karte  ho?"  
I  stayed  quiet.  
"Yahi  socha  tha."  She  smiled  knowingly.  "Tum  Osho  ki  philosophy  quote  karte  ho  aur

"Second  step?"  
"Abhi  wahan  tak  nahi  pahuncha."  
She  looked  at  me  for  a  long  second.  "Honest  ho,  kam  se  kam."  
And  it  was  the  truth.  
Another  day,  the  argument  shifted  to  Jiddu  Krishnamurti.  
"Woh  kehta  hai  ki  humein  apni  conditioning  se  azaad  hona  chahiye,"  she  argued.  "Par

"Yeh  too  convenient  hai.  Sirf  dekho  aur  free  ho  jao?"  
"Nahi.  Sirf  dekho.  Free  hone  ka  dawa  mat  karo."  
She  opened  her  mouth  to  argue,  then  stopped  herself.  
"Tumse  argue  karna  itna  annoying  kyun  hai?"  
"Kyunki  main  galat  nahi  bol  raha."  
"Isse  bhi  zyada  annoying."  
 One  afternoon,  the  energy  around  her  was  entirely  different.  Heavier.  
Chacha  brought  the  tea.  She  held  the  cup  but  didn't  take  a  sip.  
"Ek  banda  tha,"  she  started  abruptly.  "Zayan  se  pehle.  IIT  Delhi.  Do  saal."  
I  didn't  say  anything.  I  just  listened.

jo

boxes

"...Haan.  Ya  woh."  
 September  19  |  11:00  PM  
My  phone  vibrated  on  the  desk.  
It  was  the  Telegram  group:  SSC  CGL  2023  Tier-1  Result  Declared.  
I  opened  the  PDF  and  typed  in  my  roll  number.  
No  Match  Found.  
I  put  the  phone  down.  Everything  that  had  been  building  up  inside  me  for  the  last  two  
months—the
 
calculations,
 
the
 
speculations,
 
the
 
impossibly

of

in

single,
 
quiet

I  checked  the  cutoff  document.  
UR:  150.04  
A  strange,  brutal  clarity  washed  over  me.  Yes,  my  paper  hadn't  gone  well.  But  the  
game
 
had
 
also
 
evolved.
 
The
 
bar
 
had
 
been
 
raised
 
to
 
a

I  walked  up  to  the  terrace.  The  wind  was  slightly  cool.  I  expected  to  feel  an  
unbearable,
 
crushing

pressing
 
down
 
on
 
my

Instead,
 
I
 
felt

It  didn't  feel  like  an  exam  had  ended.  It  felt  like  a  long,  exhausting  race  had  concluded.  
I
 
hadn't
 
won,
 
but
 
I
 
could
 
finally
 
stop
 
running.
 
My  phone  rang.  Naina  Calling.  
I  answered  it.  
"Result  aa  gaya?"  she  asked.  
"Haan."  
"Aur?"  
"Nahi  hua."  
"Okay."  
No  suffocating  sympathy.  No  motivational  speeches.  It  was  exactly  what  I  needed.  
"Kahan  ho?"  
"Chhat  par."  
"10  minute  mein  neeche  aao."  
A  month  ago,  I  would  have  refused.  Tonight,  I  didn't.  
 The  Shaheed  Path  highway  was  completely  empty.  Naina  was  driving.  The  windows  
were
 
rolled
 
halfway
 
down,
 
letting
 
the
 
rushing
 
wind
 
fill
 
the
 
cabin.
 
"Cutoff  150  gayi  hai,"  I  said,  breaking  the  silence.  
"High?"  
"Bahut."  
She  had  the  music  on  low.  
"Theek  ho?"  she  asked  softly.  
I  honestly  checked  the  internal  gauges.  Yes.  I  was.  
"Lag  raha  hai  jaise  koi  race  khatam  ho  gayi.  Jeeta  nahi.  Par  daudna  band  ho  gaya."  
She  let  out  a  faint,  understanding  laugh  and  turned  the  volume  up  slightly.  I  leaned  my  
seat
 
back
 
and
 
closed
 
my
 
eyes.
 
Tomorrow,
 
the
 
grinding
 
reality
 
of
 
money
 
and
 
responsibilities
 
would
 
return

full
 
force.
 
But

there
 
was
 
just
 
the
 
wind.
 
 I  walked  over  to  Yadav's  momo  stall  the  next  morning.  
"Dekha?"  I  asked.  
"Haan."  He  was  mixing  chutney  with  practiced  ease.  "135  ban  rahe  the  mere.  150  toh  
alag
 
duniya
 
hai."
 
We  both  laughed.  A  dry,  humorless  chuckle.  
"Ab?"  I  asked.  
"Pata  nahi.  Thoda  rukunga."  He  stopped  mixing  and  looked  at  me.  "Ruk  le  bhai.  Par  
ruk
 
ke
 
sochna.
 
Khade-khade
 
mat
 
sochna."
 
It  wasn't  profound  philosophy.  It  was  just  straight,  hard-earned  survival  instinct.  
That  night,  Papa  called.  
"Kab  aa  raha  hai?"  he  asked.  
"Matlab?"  
"Diwali  aa  rahi  hai.  Ghar  reh  kuch  din."  
There  was  no  mention  of  the  150.04  cutoff.  No  lecture  on  wasted  years.  Just  an  
instruction
 
to
 
come
 
home.
 
"Haan.  Aa  jaunga."  
"Theek  hai."  
The  line  went  dead.  Going  home  meant  Kusumpur.  It  meant  stepping  away  from  this  
relentless
 
cycle
 
for
 
a
 
few
 
days.
 
And

first

followed

it
 
meant
 
being
 
away
 
from
 
Naina
 
for
 
a
 
few
 
days.
 
I  carefully  picked  that  thought  up  and  set  it  aside.  I  looked  out  the  window.  Everything  
was
 
exactly
 
where

was
 
supposed
 
to
 
be.

CHAPTER  13  
The  Envelope  of  Trust  
November  –  December  2023  —  Kusumpur  /  Lucknow  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
A.  The  Departure  
Morning  |  The  Bus  Stand  (Kusumpur)  
It  was  time  to  go  back  to  Lucknow.  
Maa  had  woken  me  up  early.  "Nashta  kar  le  pehle."  I  was  in  a  hurry.  She  never  was.  
As  I  was  picking  up  my  bag,  Maa  slipped  something  into  the  front  pocket  of  my  kurta  
without
 
looking
 
at
 
me.
 
"Rakh  le."  
I  sat  by  the  window  in  the  bus  and  pulled  it  out.  A  500-rupee  note.  
Maa  made  papads  with  the  village  self-help  group.  She  didn't  have  much  of  her  own.  
Yet,
 
she
 
had
 
managed
 
to
 
save
 
this.
 
That  500-rupee  note  wasn't  money.  It  was  the  physical  weight  of  her  belief  in  me.  
B.  The  Silent  Handshake  
Just  before  the  bus  started  moving,  Papa  arrived.  
He  didn't  say  a  word.  He  just  shook  my  hand  through  the  window  and  slipped  a  wad  
of
 
notes
 
into
 
my
 
palm.
 
Five
 
thousand
 
rupees.
 
"Maa  se  mat  kehna,"  he  said  gruffly.  
"Mujhe  nahi  chahiye—"  
He  looked  straight  at  me.  A  sharp,  unyielding  look.  
"Paise  ki  tension  mat  lena.  Dil  laga  ke  padhna."  
He  was  a  man  of  very  few  words.  But  when  he  spoke,  he  struck  the  nail  straight  on  the  
head.
 
I  sat  there  with  five  thousand  rupees  in  my  hand.  Sometimes,  love  arrives  disguised  
as
 
a
 
crushing
 
sense
 
of
 
responsibility.
 
C.  The  Fields  
On  the  Bus  
I  stared  out  the  window.  The  green  fields  of  Ayodhya  were  blurring  past.  
My  phone  vibrated.  A  message  from  Naina.  
“Kal  ka  din  yaad  hai  na?  26  November.  Don’t  forget.”  
CAT  2023.  The  exam  she  had  been  building  her  entire  year  around.  
I  replied:  “Yaad  hai.”  
“Nervous  hoon.  Excited  bhi.”  
“Dono  theek  hain.”  
“Tum  hamesha  itne  calm  kyun  rehte  ho?”  
“Tum  hamesha  itne  sawaal  kyun  karti  ho?”  
“Chup  raho.”  
I  smiled  faintly.  The  fields  were  ending.  The  concrete  edges  of  the  city  were  about  to  
begin.
 
 D.  The  Monologue  
Evening  |  Bhaiya's  Flat  
I  went  straight  to  the  flat.  
Pihu  was  on  the  carpet,  propped  up  with  two  pillows  behind  her  back  and  neck.  A  soft  
toy
 
of
 
Ram
 
Lala
 
was
 
placed
 
in
 
front
 
of
 
her—deliberately
 
kept
 
just
 
slightly
 
out
 
of
 
her
 
immediate
 
reach.
 
I  sat  down  on  the  floor  beside  her.  
Pihu’s  tiny  hand  reached  out  toward  the  toy.  Her  fingers  brushed  the  fabric,  but  
slipped.
 
"Dadi  ne  bheja  hai.  Ayodhya  se,"  I  told  her.  
She  didn't  respond.  She  just  kept  trying  to  reach  it.  
"Main  bhi  gaya  tha.  Saryu  dekhi.  Dadi  ne  jeb  mein  500  daale.  Unhe  lagta  hai  main  abhi  
bhi
 
10
 
saal
 
ka
 
hoon."
 
Her  hand  stretched  further.  This  time,  her  fingers  closed  around  the  edge  of  the  cloth.  
"Ek  baat  bataun?"  I  whispered.  "Aaj  pehli  baar  main  apne  kisi  paper  se  zyada  kisi  aur  
ke
 
paper
 
ke
 
liye
 
nervous
 
hoon."
 
A  breeze  blew  in  from  the  window,  rustling  the  curtain.  It  was  a  completely  one-sided  
conversation.
 
But
 
maybe
 
not
 
every
 
relationship
 
requires
 
both
 
sides
 
to
 
speak
 
in
 
order
 
to
 
be
 
understood.
 
 E.  The  Night  Before  
November  25  |  11:15  PM  
Naina  sent  a  picture  of  her  admit  card.  
“Kal  morning  slot  hai.  Wish  me  luck.”  
“Best  of  luck.”  
“Bas  itna?”  
“Kya  chahiye?”  
“Kuch  poetic.”  
I  stared  at  the  blinking  cursor.  Then  I  typed:  
“Tum  pehle  se  jaanti  ho  jo  jaanna  tha.  Kal  bas  woh  dikha  dena.”  
“…Okay  yeh  actually  sahi  tha.”  
“Ab  so  jao  Sentinel.  Mujhe  nahi,  tumhe  zaroorat  hai.”  
I  put  the  phone  down.  It  was  11:15  PM.  
Her  paper  was  tomorrow  morning.  And  she  was  right.  I  was  genuinely,  irrationally  
nervous.
 
 F.  The  Tea  
November  26  |  Morning  
I  hadn't  set  an  alarm,  yet  I  woke  up  at  exactly  7:45  AM.  
I  made  tea.  I  sat  in  my  room,  staring  at  the  wall,  tracking  the  minutes  as  her  exam  slot  
progressed.
 
At  12:30  PM,  the  message  arrived.  
“VARC  acha.  DILR  stuck.  QA  theek.”  
“Overall?”  I  asked.  
“Feel  acchi  hai.”  
“Result  January  mein  hai.  Abhi  chai  pee  lo.”  I  replied.  
“Haha.  Tum  nervous  the  na?”  
“Chai  pee  lo.”  
G.  The  Middle  Ground  
Night  |  The  Flat  
My  books  were  spread  out  on  the  table.  
Right  in  the  center  lay  Maa's  500-rupee  note  and  Papa's  5000-rupee  wad.  Both  neatly  
folded.
 
Maa's  note  was  physically  lighter,  but  it  carried  the  heaviest  emotional  weight.  Papa's  
money
 
was
 
substantial,
 
but
 
the
 
silent
 
trust
 
wrapped
 
inside
 
it
 
was
 
even
 
heavier.
 
Back  in  the  village,  Sharma  ji's  son  had  gotten  selected.  
Here  in  the  city,  Yadav  was  running  a  momo  stall  with  his  girlfriend.  
Naina  had  just  finished  the  paper  that  would  define  her  life.  
Papa  was  working  in  silence.  Maa  was  rolling  papads.  
And  me?  
I  was  suspended  somewhere  right  in  the  middle  of  all  of  them.  And  for  the  very  first  
time,
 
it
 
occurred
 
to
 
me—maybe
 
being
 
in
 
the
 
middle
 
is
 
a
 
place
 
of
 
its
 
own.

Naina's  Diary  |  26  November  2023  |  Night  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
Paper  ho  gaya.  
VARC  smooth.  DILR  mein  ek  set  kharab  gaya  —  15  minute  waste.  QA  theek  tha.  
97-98  percentile  hoga  shayad.  IIM  A  mushkil.  B  aur  C  possible.  
Yeh  sab  January  mein  pata  chalega.  
Subah  center  par  thi.  Exam  se  pehle  20  minute  bahar  khadi  rahi.  
Raat  ko  usne  kaha  tha  —  "Tum  pehle  se  jaanti  ho  jo  jaanna  tha.  Kal  bas  woh  dikha  
dena."
 
Yeh  line  subah  yaad  rahi.  Poore  paper  mein.  
Paper  ke  baad  maine  use  message  kiya.  
Usne  poocha  —  "Overall?"  
Maine  bataya.  
Usne  kaha  —  "Chai  pee  lo."  
Main  hansi.  Kyunki  —  yahi  toh  hai  woh.  
Koi  bada,  dramatic  reaction  nahi.  Koi  'itna  toh  ho  hi  jaata  hai'  wali  toxic  positivity  nahi.  
Bas
 
—
 
chai
 
pee
 
lo.

CHAPTER  14  
The  Price  of  Habit  
December  2023  —  Lucknow  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
A.  The  Quiet  Exit  
December  4  |  The  Library  
The  final  result  for  SSC  CGL  2023  was  declared.  
The  Mains  were  done.  The  typing  test  was  done.  This  was  the  final  list.  The  end  of  the  
line.
 
Sivi  was  sitting  at  her  desk  in  the  library.  The  PDF  was  open  on  her  laptop  screen.  She  
pressed
 
Ctrl+F
 
and
 
typed
 
her
 
roll
 
number.
 
No  Match  Found.  
She  closed  the  laptop.  Very  slowly.  Very  deliberately.  
There  was  no  sudden  outburst  of  anger.  No  tears.  Just  that  profound,  echoing  silence  
that
 
settles
 
in
 
when
 
you
 
realize,
 
after
 
walking
 
for
 
miles,
 
that
 
you
 
took
 
the
 
wrong
 
road.
 
I  sat  next  to  her,  completely  silent.  
We  stayed  like  that  for  a  long  time.  The  only  sound  in  the  basement  was  the  rustling  
of
 
other
 
people
 
turning
 
the
 
pages
 
of
 
their
 
books.
 
Finally,  she  picked  up  her  bag.  
"Ghar  jaaungi  kuch  din,"  she  said.  
"Haan."  
She  stopped  at  the  gate.  She  didn't  turn  around.  
"Agli  baar  karungi."  She  was  probably  saying  it  to  herself,  not  to  me.  
Then  she  walked  out.  
I  went  back  to  Seat  17.  
Seat  18  was  empty  now.  Seat  12  had  been  empty  for  months.  
The  library  doesn't  stop  people  from  dreaming.  It  just  temporarily  empties  them  out.  
When  I  went  back  to  my  room  that  night,  Maa’s  500  and  Papa’s  5000  were  still  folded  
inside
 
my
 
book.
 
I
 
hadn't
 
touched
 
them.
 
Some
 
burdens
 
aren't
 
carried
 
in
 
your
 
pocket;
 
they
 
are
 
carried
 
in
 
your
 
chest.
 
 B.  The  Logic  of  Numbers  
Naina  asked  me  about  my  mock  test  over  the  phone  a  few  days  later.  
"Aaj  ka  mock?"  
"147."  
"Attempts?"  
"82."  
"Badhao,"  she  instructed  smoothly.  "Agar  paper  easy  aaya  toh  cutoff  150  cross  
karegi.
 
90
 
touch
 
karo
 
kam
 
se
 
kam."
 
"Reasoning  mein  kahan  kate?"  
"Blood  relation.  Number  series."  
"Teen  mocks  se  yahi  hai.  Skip  karo  pehle.  End  mein  wapas  aao."  
I  noted  it  down.  There  was  no  pep  talk.  No  "Tum  kar  sakte  ho."  Just  raw  numbers  and  
pattern
 
analysis.
 
It
 
was
 
incredibly
 
helpful.
 
And
 
because
 
it
 
was
 
so
 
clinical,
 
it
 
was
 
also
 
slightly
 
uncomfortable.
 
 C.  The  Chasm  
Phoenix  Palassio  Mall  
It  was  her  idea.  
"Aaj  boring  hai.  Chal  Phoenix."  
Phoenix  Palassio.  The  part  of  Lucknow  that  doesn't  feel  like  Lucknow  at  all.  The  
moment
 
we
 
walked
 
through
 
the
 
massive
 
glass
 
doors,

was

was
 
her

Naina  walked  through  the  glittering  corridors  with  absolute,  natural  ease.  She  walked  
into

browsed
 
through

I  took  it  from  her.  The  very  first  thing  my  eyes  caught  was  the  small  cardboard  tag

from
 
the
 
sleeve.
 
₹5,990.  
Four  months  of  my  library  fees.  
I  handed  it  right  back  to  her.  "Nahi  chahiye."  
"Try  karne  se  khareedna  compulsory  thodi  hai,"  she  argued  lightly.  
"Mujhe  aadat  nahi  dalni  us  cheez  ki  jo  main  afford  nahi  kar  sakta."  
She  looked  at  me  for  a  long  second.  She  didn't  force  it.  She  just  put  the  jacket  back  on  
the
 
rack.
 
But  that  tiny  moment—when  she  extended  the  jacket,  and  I  pulled  my  hand  back  after

 D.  The  Food  Court  
We  sat  at  a  table  in  the  sprawling  food  court.  
"Kya  loge?"  she  asked.  
"Chai  hai?"  
She  laughed.  "Mall  mein  chai.  Chaayos  chalte  hain."  
We  ordered.  She  was  talking  about  the  jacket,  the  fabric,  the  winter  collection.  I  was  
just
 
listening.
 
Or
 
pretending
 
to.
 
Then  she  stopped.  "Tum  aaj  quiet  ho."  
"Hamesha  quiet  hoon."  
"Alag  wali  quiet  hai  aaj."  
I  gripped  my  paper  tea  cup.  "Kuch  nahi."  
She  looked  at  me  intently  for  a  second.  Then,  she  let  it  go.  That  was  the  thing  about  
her—she
 
never
 
pushed.

 E.  The  Gravity  
The  Drop-off  
It  was  night.  
In  the  car,  she  was  humming  a  song  under  her  breath,  just  for  herself.  I  was  staring  
out
 
the
 
window
 
at
 
the
 
city
 
lights
 
reflecting
 
off
 
the
 
Gomti.
 
"Utro?"  she  asked  playfully  as  the  car  stopped  outside  my  building.  
"Haan."  
I  unbuckled  my  seatbelt.  
"Aaj  acha  laga?"  she  asked.  
"Haan."  
"Jhooth."  
I  stopped  with  my  hand  on  the  door  handle.  
"Kuch  tha.  Main  jaanti  hoon.  Par  theek  hai,"  she  said  softly.  
I  pushed  the  door  open.  "Good  night  Naina."  
"Good  night  Sentinel."  
The  car  drove  away.  
I  stood  there,  trying  to  untangle  what  exactly  was  knotting  up  inside  me.  It  wasn't  just  
about
 
refusing
 
to
 
try
 
on
 
an
 
expensive
 
jacket.
 
The

wasn't
 
that
 
I
 
couldn't
 
afford
 
it.
 
The
 
terrifying
 
truth
 
was
 
that
 
I
 
had
 
liked
 
the
 
jacket.
 
I  wanted  to  remain  Eklavya.  I  wanted  to  stay  on  my  own  difficult,  unglamorous  path.  
But  her  gravity  was  increasing.  Slowly.  Without  making  a  sound,  she  was  pulling  me  
into
 
her
 
orbit.
 
She
 
wasn't
 
doing
 
it
 
deliberately.
 
She
 
was
 
just
 
being
 
herself.
 
And
 
that
 
was
 
the
 
most
 
terrifying
 
part
 
of
 
all.
 
 F.  The  Incomplete  Chapter  
Night  |  The  Terrace  
I  stood  on  the  terrace.  The  December  chill  had  begun  to  set  in.  
Naina  would  get  her  CAT  results  in  January.  If  she  converted  an  IIM,  she  would  leave.  
Campus.
 
Hostels.
 
A
 
new
 
city.
 
A
 
new
 
stratosphere.
 
Earlier,  my  fear  had  been  that  she  would  leave.  
Now,  my  fear  was  that  before  she  left,  I  was  slowly  becoming  something  I  never  
intended
 
to
 
be.
 
I  was  becoming  a  temporary  station  on  her  journey.  And  she  was  becoming  an  
unfinished,
 
lingering
 
chapter
 
in
 
mine.
 
We  couldn't  possibly  exist  together  in  the  long  run.  
Or  could  we?  
I  didn't  know.  
I  stood  in  the  cold  for  a  very  long  time.

 B.  The  Library  Gate

 C.  Kapoorthala  Chowk

 D.  The  Sky  Bar  |  Night  
Renaissance.  The  same  place.

 E.  11:00  PM  |  The  Car  
She  was  driving.  I  was  in  the  passenger  seat.  
Lucknow  at  night.  The  biting  chill  of  December.  
A  short  distance  away  from  her  house,  she  pulled  the  car  over  to  the  side  of  the  
empty
 
road.
 
She
 
didn't
 
turn
 
the
 
engine
 
off.
 
"Kaisi  thi  party?"  she  asked,  looking  straight  ahead.  
"Sahi  thi."  
She  turned  toward  me.  
"Sach  bolo.  Aankhon  mein  dekh  ke."  
I  looked  at  her.  
"Tumhe  nahi  laga  sahi,"  she  answered  her  own  question.  "Main  jaanti  thi."  
"Phir  bhi  leke  gayi,"  I  pointed  out.  
"Kyunki  main  chahti  thi  tum  wahan  ho."  
There  was  no  wind  outside.  The  world  had  gone  entirely  still  for  a  second.  
She  leaned  slightly  toward  me.  It  wasn't  a  physical  step—it  was  that  microscopic,  
undeniable
 
shift
 
that
 
occurs
 
when
 
someone
 
finally
 
makes
 
a
 
decision.
 
Her  breath  mingled  with  mine.  
I  was  there.  Completely.  Not  a  single  fraction  of  me  was  standing  outside  the  car  or  
observing
 
from
 
a
 
distance.
 
And  for  the  very  first  time,  I  understood  what  it  actually  meant  to  be  with  someone.  
It  was  just  a  moment.  But  a  single  moment  can  hold  an  entire  world  inside  it.  
 F.  The  Aftermath  
She  dropped  me  home.  
The  lights  were  on  inside  my  flat.  
She  drove  away.  I  stood  there  on  the  street  for  a  long  time.  
Something  had  shifted  inside  me—not  the  part  that  was  afraid,  but  the  part  that  had  
finally
 
opened
 
up
 
for
 
the
 
first
 
time.
 
And
 
that
 
was
 
exactly
 
why
 
it
 
was
 
so
 
terrifying.
 
For  the  next  ten  days,  I  avoided  her  messages.  
She  sent  them.  I  read  them.  I  didn't  reply.  
Once,  she  wrote:  "Theek  ho?"  
Three  days  later,  I  typed:  "Haan."  
She  knew  something  had  happened.  I  let  her  know.  But  I  didn't  explain  it.  
Because  to  explain  something  to  someone  else,  you  first  have  to  understand  it  
yourself.
 
And  I  wasn't  there  yet.

99.  Ho  gaya.  
Mummy  ki  awaaz  sunke  roya.  Woh  nahi  suni  —  bas  unka  happy  hona  kaafi  tha.

hain.

Usne  kuch  khaas  nahi  kaha.  "Haan.  Haan."  Par  woh  wahan  tha.  Poora.  
Hug  karna  chahti  thi.  Nahi  kiya.  Theek  tha  yeh  bhi.  
Long  distance  waala  sawaal  kyun  poocha  —  pata  nahi.  Achanak  aaya.

Sheher  chal  raha  tha.  Woh  nahi.  
Phir  usne  kaha,  "Ok."  
Ek  minute  ki  woh  chuppi  —  usne  kuch  socha.  Mujhe  nahi  pata  kya.  Par  socha.  
Car  mein  raat  ko  usne  meri  aankhon  mein  dekha.  
Main  yeh  nahi  likhungi  kya  hua.  
Kuch  cheezein  likhne  se  chhoti  ho  jaati  hain.  
Bas  itna  —  pehli  baar  laga  ki  woh  wahan  tha.  Poora.  Koi  hissa  bahar  nahi  tha.  
Aur  pehli  baar  dar  laga  mujhe  bhi.  
10  din  se  reply  nahi  kar  raha.

A.  Ten  Days  
She  messaged.  
I  saw  it.

It  was  the  very  first  time  I  had  deliberately  ignored  her  message.  I  had  never  felt  the

to
 
before.
 
But  after  that  night—in  the  car,  that  specific  moment—something  fundamental  had  
shifted
 
inside
 
me.
 
I
 
couldn't
 
understand
 
it.
 
I
 
couldn't
 
process
 
it.
 
So,
 
I
 
stayed
 
quiet.
 
I  went  to  the  library.  I  studied.  At  night,  I  went  up  to  the  terrace.  I  thought  about  it.  
Nothing
 
resolved
 
itself.
 
“Theek  ho?”  she  asked.  
Three  days  later,  I  finally  typed:  “Haan.”  
She  went  quiet  too.  
 B.  The  First  Time  
January  |  The  Tapri  
She  was  already  at  the  tapri  when  I  arrived.  
I  looked  at  her.  She  was  looking  straight  at  me.  She  didn't  even  wait  for  me  to  sit  
down.
 
"Avoid  kar  rahe  the  mujhe."  
"Nahi—"  
"Eklavya."  
I  stopped.  "Haan."  
Chacha  brought  the  tea  and  placed  the  two  glasses  on  the  bench  between  us.  
"Kyun?"  she  asked.  
One  second.  Two  seconds.  
"Process  kar  raha  tha."  
"Kya?"  
"Jo  hua.  Car  mein."  
She  kept  looking  at  me.  A  direct,  unblinking  gaze.  
"Woh  real  tha.  Main  thi.  Tum  the.  Jo  tha  —  real  tha.  Isme  tumhe  guilt  nahi  hona  
chahiye."
 
I  didn't  say  anything.  
"Tum  apni  har  cheez  itni  zyada  andar  rakhte  ho  ki  kabhi  kabhi  main  sochti  hoon  —  
yeh
 
insaan
 
saans
 
kaise
 
leta
 
hai,"
 
she
 
said
 
softly.
 
A  faint  smile  touched  my  lips.  I  didn't  even  know  why.  
"Woh  bhi  real  tha  —  yeh,"  she  pointed  out,  gesturing  to  my  smile.  
I  drank  my  tea.  Something  tight  inside  my  chest  finally  began  to  settle.  
 C.  The  Teacher  
"Mock  score  kya  hai?"  she  asked  suddenly.  
"151."  
"English  mein?"  
"32."  
She  frowned.  "Itna  hi?  Yeh  weak  hai  tumhari."  
"Haan."  
"Kya  padhte  ho  English  mein?"  
"Newspapers.  Kabhi  kabhi."  
"'Kabhi  kabhi'  se  kaam  nahi  chalega,"  she  declared,  slipping  instantly  into  her

"Roz
 
ek

carry
 
karta
 
hai."
 
I  stayed  quiet,  just  listening.  
"Kal  se  shuru  karte  hain.  Ek  editorial  roz.  Tum  padhoge,  mujhe  summary  doge."  
"Tum  teacher  ban  rahi  ho?"  I  asked  mildly.  
"Tum  student  ban  rahe  ho.  Fark  hai,"  she  shot  back.  
 D.  The  Return  
The  Library  
One  morning,  there  was  a  bag  on  Seat  18.  
Sivi.  
She  was  back.  
Our  eyes  met.  I  gave  her  a  small  nod.  Before  sitting  down,  she  looked  at  me  and  said  
very
 
quietly:
 
"Agli  baar  karungi.  Pakka."  
"Haan."  
She  sat  down.  Opened  her  notes.  Her  pen  started  moving  across  the  paper.  
Nothing  had  changed  in  the  basement.  And  yet,  absolutely  everything  had  changed.  
 E.  Breathing  
Night  |  The  Flat  
I  was  walking  past  the  living  room.  
Pihu  was  in  Bhabhi’s  lap.  Bhabhi  was  singing  something  to  her—a  soft,  low  lullaby.  
Pihu’s
 
eyes
 
were
 
closed.
 
I  stopped  at  the  doorway.  
This  was  a  scene  that  happened  every  single  day.  But  I  had  never  truly  watched  it,  
because
 
I
 
usually
 
locked
 
myself
 
in
 
my
 
room
 
by
 
this
 
time.
 
Today,
 
I
 
stopped.
 
Bhabhi  looked  up  and  saw  me.  She  didn't  say  anything.  I  stood  there  for  a  long  time.  
A  few  days  ago,  Naina  had  asked  me— "Tum  saans  kaise  lete  ho."  
Looking  at  Pihu  sleeping  peacefully  in  her  mother's  arms,  I  finally  understood  the  
answer.
 
I
 
do
 
breathe.
 
I
 
just
 
do
 
it
 
in
 
entirely
 
different
 
places.
 
 F.  The  Routine  
I  read  the  editorial.  The  Hindu.  It  was  about  a  new  economic  policy.  
I  drafted  a  summary  and  sent  it  to  Naina.  
Three  minutes  later,  my  phone  vibrated.  
"Argument  theek  pakda.  Par  conclusion  miss  kiya.  Author  kehta  hai  policy  fail  hogi  —  
tumne
 
sirf
 
describe
 
kiya
 
ki
 
kya
 
hai."
 
"Dobara  padhta  hoon."  
"Haan."  
It  became  a  routine.  
Every  day,  one  editorial.  Every  day,  one  summary.  Every  day,  one  correction.  
No  drama.  No  motivational  speeches.  Just—every  day.  
And  inside  this  "every  day,"  something  was  quietly  taking  root  that  hadn't  been  there  
before.
 
It
 
was
 
comfort.
 
The  very  thing  I  was  terrified  of  was  slowly  starting  to  feel  like  home.  And  that—that  
was
 
the
 
most
 
terrifying
 
part
 
of
 
all.

CHAPTER  16  
The  River's  Promise  
March  2024  —  Lucknow  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
A.  The  Evening  
It  was  her  idea.  
"Chal  bahar.  Gomti  par."  
Her  interview  for  IIM  Bangalore  was  scheduled  for  March  9th.  It  was  exactly  six  days  
away.
 
We  sat  on  the  concrete  steps  of  the  riverfront.  The  sun  was  beginning  to  set,  casting  
long,
 
fractured
 
streaks
 
of
 
burnt
 
orange
 
across
 
the
 
surface
 
of
 
the
 
water.
 
The  river  kept  flowing—exactly  the  way  it  always  did,  indifferent  to  whoever  sat  on  its  
banks.
 
B.  The  Confession  
She  remained  quiet  for  a  long  time.  Then,  she  spoke.  
"Tumse  milne  se  pehle  —  main  akele  nahi  reh  sakti  thi.  Matlab  physically  akele.  
Hamesha
 
koi
 
chahiye
 
tha.
 
Ananya.
 
Kabeer.
 
Koi
 
bhi."
 
She  stared  out  at  the  dark  water.  
"Drinking  thi.  Smoking  thi.  Overconfident  thi  —  ya  kam  se  kam  dikhti  thi.  Aur  ek  
cheez...
 
bahut
 
door
 
jaana
 
tha.
 
Kisi
 
badi
 
company
 
ki
 
CEO
 
banna
 
tha.
 
Kahan?
 
Pata
 
nahi.
 
Bas
 
door."
 
"Door  matlab?"  I  asked  softly.  
"Yahan  se.  Sab  se."  
She  paused,  letting  the  evening  breeze  fill  the  silence.  
"Par  tumhare  saath  —  ek  bench  par  baith  ke  bhi  —  itna  sukoon  kyun  milta  hai?  Yeh  
samajh
 
nahi
 
aata
 
mujhe."
 
The  river  kept  moving.  
"Tum  kya  chahte  ho  Sentinel?  Sach  mein?"  
For  a  fraction  of  a  second,  I  retreated  into  myself.  Then,  I  was  there.  Entirely  present.  
"Koi  pahad  ho,"  I  said  quietly.  "Jahan  koi  na  ho.  Sirf  main  hoon.  Aur  wahan  —  khud  se  
milun.
 
Koi
 
naam
 
nahi.
 
Koi
 
result
 
nahi.
 
Koi
 
prove
 
karna
 
nahi.
 
Bas
 
—
 
woh
 
jo
 
main
 
hoon,
 
ussse
 
milun."
 
She  stayed  quiet.  
"Kabhi  kabhi  sab  chhod  dene  ka  mann  karta  hai,"  I  confessed,  the  words  slipping  out  
easier
 
than
 
I
 
expected.
 
"Isme
 
aur
 
usme
 
nahi
 
—
 
bas
 
in
 
sab
 
se.
 
Aur
 
khud
 
mein
 
ghus
 
jaun."
 
The  sun  dipped  lower  beneath  the  horizon.  
The  water  turned  a  shade  deeper,  swallowing  the  orange  reflections.  
That  was  when  she  held  my  hand.  
It  wasn't  sudden  or  forceful.  She  slid  her  hand  into  mine  very  gently,  almost  as  if  she  
were
 
quietly
 
asking—
is
 
this
 
okay?
 
"Sentinel."  
"Haan."  
"Main  tumhe  ab  kabhi  nahi  chhodungi."  
I  didn't  say  a  single  word.  
The  river  flowed  in  front  of  us.  
Her  hand  remained  wrapped  in  mine.  
Deep  inside,  something  twisted.  There  was  a  part  of  me  that  was  utterly  terrified  of  
what
 
this
 
meant.
 
And
 
there
 
was
 
another
 
part
 
that
 
wanted
 
it
 
more
 
than
 
anything
 
else
 
in
 
the
 
world.
 
They  both  existed  at  exactly  the  same  time.  
C.  The  Return  
It  was  dark  by  the  time  we  walked  back.  
We  stood  outside  her  house,  near  her  car.  
"Bangalore  ki  taiyaari?"  I  asked.  
"Kal  se."  
"Theek  hai."  
She  walked  through  the  gates.  
I  stood  there  on  the  empty  street.  My  hand  was  still  carrying  the  lingering  warmth  of  
her
 
fingers.
 
This  was  a  bond.  It  was  real,  and  it  was  tying  me  down.  And  for  the  very  first  time  in  
my
 
life,
 
I
 
didn't
 
want
 
to
 
run
 
away
 
from
 
it.
 
The  fear  was  still  there.  It  hadn't  left.  But  it  had  taken  on  a  slightly  different  shape  
now.

CHAPTER  17  
The  Unfolding  
April  –  September  2024  —  Lucknow  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
A.  The  Result  |  April  2024  
The  message  arrived  in  the  afternoon.  
"IIM  Lucknow.  Confirm."  
I  was  in  the  library.  Seat  17.  Sivi  was  at  Seat  18.  
She  glanced  at  my  screen,  looked  at  my  face,  and  instantly  understood  that  
something
 
had
 
happened.
 
"Theek  hai?"  she  asked.  
"Haan.  Naina  ka  IIM  result  aaya."  
She  nodded  and  went  straight  back  to  her  notes.  She  didn't  ask  anything  else.  That  
was
 
the
 
right
 
thing
 
to
 
do.
 
I  met  Naina  at  the  tapri  in  the  evening.  
She  was  radiating  happiness—not  the  loud,  performative  kind,  but  a  deep,  quiet  joy  
that
 
simply
 
glowed
 
from
 
the
 
inside
 
out.
 
"Lucknow  hai.  Yahin  hoon,"  she  said  directly,  looking  into  my  eyes.  
I  took  a  sip  of  my  tea.  
"Campus  mein  shift  ho  jao,"  I  said  evenly.  
She  stopped.  Her  smile  faltered  slightly.  
"Matlab?"  
"Hostel  life  MBA  ka  hissa  hai.  Jo  wahan  hoga  —  dinners,  group  work,  raat  ko  
discussions
 
—
 
woh
 
bahar
 
se
 
nahi
 
milega."
 
"Main  yahin  se  bhi—"  
"Naina."  
She  went  completely  silent.  
"Poora  experience  lo.  Andar  rehkar,"  I  told  her.  
She  kept  looking  at  me.  Her  gaze  was  heavy,  searching  for  the  underlying  motive.  
"Tum  mujhe  door  kar  rahe  ho,"  she  said,  her  voice  dropping.  
I  placed  my  tea  cup  on  the  bench.  
"Main  tumhe  tumhara  experience  dene  ki  koshish  kar  raha  hoon."  
She  stayed  quiet  for  a  long  second,  processing  the  boundaries  I  was  drawing.  
"Theek  hai,"  she  said  softly.  "Par  yeh  tumhari  choice  hai.  Meri  nahi."  
 B.  The  Departure  |  June  
Her  orientation  was  on  June  3rd.  
She  came  to  see  me  the  day  before  she  shifted.  She  sat  down  on  the  floor  next  to  
Pihu.
 
Pihu
 
had
 
recently
 
started
 
holding
 
hands
 
for
 
slightly
 
longer
 
intervals.
 
Naina  extended  her  hand.  Pihu’s  tiny  fingers  wrapped  around  Naina's  index  
finger—just
 
for
 
one
 
second.
 
Naina  looked  up  at  me.  
Neither  of  us  said  a  word.  
Later,  as  she  was  leaving,  we  stood  at  the  gate  of  my  building.  
"Messages  karta  rehna,"  she  instructed.  
"Haan."  
"Sentinel."  
"Haan."  
"Main  yahan  hoon.  Sirf  20  minute  door."  
"Pata  hai."  
Her  car  drove  away.  
I  stood  there  by  the  gate.  I  knew  pushing  her  to  the  campus  was  the  right  thing  to  do.  I  
was
 
absolutely
 
certain
 
of
 
it.
 
Yet,  there  was  a  heavy  knot  in  my  chest—the  kind  of  feeling  that  carries  the  agonizing  
weight
 
of
 
holding
 
two
 
entirely
 
conflicting
 
truths
 
at
 
exactly
 
the
 
same
 
time.
 
 C.  The  Grind  |  July  –  August  
The  Library.  
8:00  AM  every  morning.  Seat  17.  Sivi  at  Seat  18.  
This  was  a  routine  that  couldn't  be  explained  to  an  outsider.  It  simply  existed.  
Naina's  messages  arrived  at  different  times  now—mostly  late  at  night.  She  was  living  
the
 
campus
 
life.
 
Case
 
studies.
 
Group
 
dinners.
 
Late-night
 
discussions.
 
"Aaj  ka  mock?"  she  texted.  
"153."  
"Attempt?"  
"89."  
"Blood  relation?"  
"Skip  kiya.  3  minute  bachaye."  
"Theek  hai."  
One  day,  during  our  tea  break,  Sivi  turned  to  me.  
"Ek  cheez  notice  ki  hai.  Tum  September  se  pehle  wale  tum  nahi  hote.  Concentrate  
zyada

"Achha  hai  na?"  
"Haan.  Par  kahin  kuch  aur  bhi  hai."  
I  didn't  respond.  She  stayed  quiet  too.  We  both  knew  the  conversation  had  reached  its  
natural
 
end
 
right
