 D.  Pihu  |  Late  August  
It  was  the  last  week  of  August.  
Vikram  bhaiya  came  out  of  the  room  with  a  rare  smile  on  his  face.  "Aaj  usne  khud  ball  
uthaayi.
 
Bhabhi
 
ke
 
haath
 
se."
 
I  walked  into  the  room.  
Pihu  was  sleeping.  The  Ram  Lala  soft  toy  was  tucked  near  her  chest,  held  in  a  loose,  
delicate
 
grip.
 
I  sat  down  on  the  floor  beside  her  and  just  watched  her  breathe.  
"Pihu,"  I  whispered  softly.  
She  was  deep  in  her  sleep.  
"September  mein  paper  hai.  Phir  result.  Phir  pata  nahi."  
The  ceiling  fan  hummed  a  steady  rhythm  above  us.  
"Par  tum  ho.  Aur  yeh  kaafi  hai  aaj  ke  liye."  
I  sat  there  in  the  quiet  room  for  a  long  time.  
 E.  The  Night  Before  |  September  8  
At  9:00  PM,  my  phone  rang.  Papa.  
"Haan  Papa."  
"Kal  hai?"  
"Haan.  26  ko.  Doosri  shift."  
He  didn't  say  anything  for  a  second.  The  silence  on  the  line  was  thick.  
"Theek  se  jaana.  Jaldi  nikalna."  
"Haan."  
"Khaana  kha  ke  sona."  
"Ji."  
He  cut  the  call.  
Three  lines.  That  was  all.  
But  inside  those  three  simple  lines,  I  could  hear  the  grinding  weight  of  three  entire  
years.
 
Everything
 
he
 
had
 
never
 
said
 
out
 
loud,
 
everything
 
I
 
knew
 
he
 
felt,
 
was
 
neatly
 
folded
 
into
 
those
 
instructions.
 
At  10:00  PM,  Naina  called.  
"Kal  paper  hai."  
"Haan."  
"Nervous  ho?"  
"Thoda."  
She  let  out  a  soft,  echoing  laugh.  
"Tum  pehle  se  jaante  ho  jo  jaanna  tha.  Kal  bas  woh  dikha  dena."  
The  exact  same  words.  The  ones  I  had  texted  her  the  night  before  her  CAT  exam.  
"Yeh  meri  line  hai,"  I  pointed  out.  
"Tumhari  line  tumhare  paas  wapas."  She  paused.  "Good  night  Sentinel."  
"Good  night."  
Sleep  didn't  come  easily  that  night.  
Three  years.  Seat  17.  The  0.75  margin.  A  paper  that  had  slipped  out  of  my  hands.  
Papa's  three  lines.  Naina's  echoed  words.  
Everything  was  assembled  right  there  in  the  dark  room  with  me.  
Tomorrow  was  the  day  of  unfolding.

But  at  night  —  he  is  only  twenty  minutes  away.  
Sometimes  it  feels  like  he  is  right  here,  next  to  me.  
 
Sometimes
 
it
 
feels
 
like
 
he
 
isn’t
 
here
 
at
 
all.
 
Both  feelings  feel  completely  real.  
Tomorrow’s  paper  is  three  years  of  his  work.  
He  missed  it  once  by  0.75  marks.  
Tomorrow  he  shouldn’t  miss  it.  
"Tum  pehle  se  jaante  ho  jo  jaanna  tha."  
He  really  does  know  it.  
Tomorrow  he  just  has  to  show  it.  
—  N.

A.  The  Night  Before  
The  room  light  was  off.  
I  lay  on  the  bed.  Eyes  closed.  Face  turned  toward  the  wall.  
Sleep  should  have  come.  Tomorrow  mattered.  
But  the  mind  was  loud.  
Five  minutes.  Fifteen  minutes.  
I  got  up.  
Picked  up  the  phone.  Put  Osho  on  speaker  —  Ashtavakra  Gita.  
"Tum  wahi  ho  jo  dekh  raha  hai.  Karta  tum  nahi  ho."  
The  philosophy  was  fine.  
But  if  the  system  hung  tomorrow  —  who  would  be  the  doer?  
At  2  a.m.  I  went  to  the  terrace.  
There  was  a  slight  chill  in  the  September  night.  The  city  lights  were  far  away.  
Naina’s  hostel  was  twenty  minutes  from  there.  
The  lights  there  would  be  off.  She  would  be  sleeping.  
And  me?  
I  was  still  the  same  thing.  
An  aspirant.  
The  word  sounded  different  now.  
Aspirant  —  someone  who  hasn’t  become  anything  yet.  Someone  who  is  still  trying.  
I  went  back  inside.  
Played  Parmar  Sir’s  audio.  Polity.  
Somewhere  around  3  a.m.,  while  listening,  sleep  came.  
 
B.  The  Morning  |  4:30  
Bhaiya  knocked  on  the  door.  
"Chal.  Nikalna  hai."  
My  eyes  opened.  Heavy.  
I  drank  water.  Washed  my  face.  Looked  in  the  mirror.  
My  eyes  were  red.  Slightly  swollen.  
Got  ready.  
Kurta.  Jeans.  Bag.  
Before  leaving,  I  glanced  into  Pihu’s  room.  
She  was  sleeping  beside  Bhabhi.  
In  sleep  her  hand  moved  suddenly  —  for  no  reason.  
In  my  mind  I  said:  
“Tere  liye.”  
At  5  a.m.  the  Swift  left.  
Bhaiya  in  the  driver’s  seat.  
The  radio  hummed  faintly.  
He  didn’t  ask  the  obvious  question  —  
"Taiyaari  kaisi  hai?"  
He  knew  the  question  was  useless.  
He  just  drove.  
Morning  light  began  spreading  over  the  highway.  
Fields  appeared.  Farmers  appeared.  Ploughing.  
They  know  what  they’re  doing.  
Seeds  go  in.  Crops  come  out.  
My  process?  
Give  the  paper.  
Face
 
glitches.
 
Wait
 
for
 
the
 
result.
 
The  farmer  was  clearer  than  me.  
My  phone  buzzed.  
Naina.  
"Chai  mili?"  
"Raaste  mein  hoon."  
"All  the  best.  Ladna  mat  system  se.  Bas  paper  dena."  
She  knew.  
She  always  knew.  
 
C.  The  Centre  |  10:30  
There  was  a  crowd  outside.  
Same  faces.  Same  fear.  
People  flipping  through  notes.  
My  seat  was  in  the  corner.  
I  turned  on  the  computer.  
Moved  the  mouse.  
The  cursor  lagged.  
Click.  Response  late.  
Click  again.  Still  late.  
I  called  the  invigilator.  
"Sir.  Mouse  glitch  kar  raha  hai."  
He  checked.  
"Chal  toh  raha  hai."  
"Sir  smooth  nahi  hai."  
He  took  out  a  strip  of  tape.  
Stuck  it  under  the  mouse.  
A  fix.  
"Ab  theek  hai.  Change  nahi  hoga."  
I  remembered  Bhaiya.  
I  remembered  Pihu.  
I  swallowed  the  anger.  
11:45.  Paper  started.  
Timer:  60:00  
Q1.  GK.  Easy.  
Q5.  Reasoning.  
The  screen  froze.  
Cursor  disappeared.  
Ten  seconds.  
Twenty  seconds.  
Thirty  seconds.  
The  screen  returned.  
My  focus  had  slipped.  
Q15.  
Freeze  again.  
This  time  forty  seconds.  
Naina  had  said  —  
"Ladna  mat  system  se."  
But  the  system  was  fighting  me.  
I  took  a  deep  breath.  
To  hell  with  the  mouse.  
I’ll  do  what  I  know.  
Last  five  minutes  —  fast.  
Timer  zero.  
"Your  exam  has  been  submitted."  
 
D.  The  Return  |  12:45  
I  stepped  outside.  
Sunlight  hit  my  eyes  sharply.  
Bhaiya  stood  near  the  car,  talking  on  the  phone.  
He  saw  me.  
Cut  the  call.  
Held  out  a  water  bottle.  
"Kaisa  gaya?"  
I  drank.  
The  lump  in  my  throat  went  down.  
Should  I  tell  him?  
That  the  mouse  was  bad?  
That  the  system  froze  three  times?  
No.  
He  had  driven  a  hundred  kilometers.  
He  didn’t  need  complaints.  
He  needed  hope.  
"Theek  tha.  Pre  clear  ho  jayega."  
Not  a  lie.  
But  not  the  full  truth  either.  
I  took  out  my  phone.  
"Done?"  
"Haan.  Glitch  tha.  Mouse  issue.  Par  attempt  theek  hain."  
"Ghar  pahunch  ke  call  karna.  Ab  sochna  band."  
The  car  started.  
I  leaned  back  in  the  seat.  
Bhaiya  turned  on  the  radio.  
Kanpur  was  fading  behind.  
Lucknow  was  ahead.  
And  I  —  
was  still  in  between.  
Still.

My  first  thought  was  anger.  At  the  system.

“Sentinel.”

A  bad  paper.

The  library  from  morning  to  evening.  
Today  he  stood  between  Kanpur  and  Lucknow.  
An  aspirant.  
But  the  strange  thing  is  —  the  way  he  lives  in  that  in-between  space…  
that  too  is  a  kind  of  being.

There  are  weeks.

A.  8  October  |  Morning  
I  woke  up  and  checked  my  phone.  
There  was  a  message  from  Naina  —  sent  at  midnight.  
“Happy  birthday  Sentinel.  Kal  milte  hain.”  
Twenty-six.  
The  fan  moved  slowly  above  me.  Outside  the  October  sunlight  had  already  filled  the  
room.
 
From  inside  the  house  Bhaiya  called  out,  
“Chai  ban  rahi  hai.”  
Then  I  heard  Pihu’s  laughter.  
It  was  a  new  sound.  
It  had  started  only  a  few  weeks  ago.  
The  day  began.  
 
B.  Campus  
Sitapur  Road  —  outside  the  city.  
The  moment  we  reached  the  gate  it  became  clear  that  this  place  was  different.  
Red  brick  buildings.  Large  gates.  Green  lawns.  
Two  hundred  acres.  
Completely  separate  from  the  noise  of  the  city.  
Naina  was  inside.  
She  met  me  at  the  gate.  
“Aao.”  
We  walked.  
She  kept  explaining  things.  
Students  moved  around  holding  case  files.  
Some  sat  in  groups  discussing  something.  
Someone  sat  alone  with  a  laptop  in  the  corner.  
Everything  here  had  direction.  
Everyone  seemed  to  be  building  something.  
I  just  watched.  
This  was  her  place.  
You  could  tell  immediately.  
 
C.  Cafeteria  
We  sat  down.  
Naina  started  explaining  something  —  group  project,  presentation.  
Just  then  a  boy  walked  in.  
“Naina.  Hi.”  
He  was  tall.  
There  was  a  specific  kind  of  confidence  about  him  —  the  kind  that  comes  from  IIT  
campuses.
 
“Eklavya,”  Naina  introduced  me.  
 
“Arjun.
 
Batchmate.”
 
He  shook  my  hand.  
Looked  at  me  for  a  second.  
“You  are  lucky.”  
Then  he  turned  toward  Naina  and  looked  straight  into  her  eyes.  
“Love  is  a  feeling.  But  survival  is  a  competition  —  and  I  always  win.”  
For  a  moment  there  was  silence.  
I  said  one  word.  
“Inevitable.”  
Arjun  looked  at  me  again.  
Then  smiled.  
And  walked  away.  
Naina  looked  at  me.  
Neither  of  us  said  anything.  
We  finished  our  tea.  
 
D.  The  Lawn  
On  the  way  back  we  stopped  on  the  lawn.  
October  sunlight  —  the  kind  that  doesn’t  burn.  It  simply  exists.  
“Kaisa  laga?”  she  asked.  
“Tumhari  jagah  hai.”  
“Tumhari  jagah?”  
I  stayed  quiet  for  a  moment.  
Then  I  said,  
“Koi  pahad.  Koi  nadi.  Jahan  koi  na  ho.  Bas  main  hoon  —  khud  se  milun.  Koi  result  
nahi.
 
Koi
 
prove
 
karna
 
nahi.”
 
She  stayed  silent.  
Then  —  
She  placed  a  hand  on  my  shoulder.  
Gently.  
She  moved  closer.  
It  wasn’t  exactly  a  hug.  
Something  else.  
That  warmth  which  appears  when  someone  silently  decides  —  main  yahan  hoon.  
And  for  one  moment  —  just  one  —  
there  was  the  faint  closeness  of  lips  near  lips.  
No  words.  
The  trees  on  the  lawn  moved  in  the  wind.  
She  stepped  back.  
Looked  at  me.  
Her  eyes  weren’t  calm.  
They  were  controlled.  
But  they  were  there.  
I  didn’t  say  anything  either.  
Some  moments  become  smaller  the  moment  you  try  to  explain  them  with  words.  
 
E.  Return  
We  took  an  auto  back.  
Slowly  the  city  returned  around  us.  
The  campus  disappeared  behind.  
The  result  date  was  still  unknown.  
According  to  the  answer  key  my  score  was  148.  
There  was  a  chance.  
But  not  the  kind  of  confidence  that  should  exist.  
For  now  there  was  only  waiting.  
I  returned  to  my  room.  
Pihu  was  asleep.  
Bhaiya  didn’t  ask  anything.  
Twenty-six  years.  
And  that  moment  on  the  lawn  —  
was  still  somewhere  inside  
NAINA  —  DIARY  
8  October  2024  |  Night  
Today  was  his  birthday.  
I  didn’t  smoke.  
I  didn’t  drink.  
He  didn’t  notice.  
I  didn’t  tell  him.  
It  just  happened.  
On  the  lawn  he  spoke  about  mountains.  A  river.  Being  alone.  
He  truly  wants  that.  
And  in  that  moment  —  I  don’t  know  why  —  I  couldn’t  stop  myself.  
I  hugged  him.  
And  for  a  moment  —  that  moment.  
He  didn’t  say  anything.  
Neither  did  I.  
Because  some  things  stop  existing  the  moment  you  try  to  say  them.  
Arjun’s  line  —  
“Love  is  a  feeling.  But  survival  is  a  competition  —  and  I  always  win.”  
Eklavya  said  only  one  word  —  
“Inevitable.”  
Arjun  had  no  answer.  
Neither  did  I  for  that  moment.  
But  inside  there  was  something  —  
that  feeling  which  appears  when  someone  stands  for  you  without  saying  anything.  
Without  planning  it.  
The  result  will  come  whenever  it  comes.  
148  is  borderline.  
He  knows  it.  
I  know  it.  
Both  of  us  are  quiet.  
That  too  is  a  kind  of  conversation.  
—  N.

I  cut  the  call.

Freedom  or  escape.

The  place  that  belongs  only  to  him.

Because  I  knew  this  day  would  come.

“This  diary  is  for  that.  So  you  don’t  forget  me.  What  we  had  —  it  was  real.”

The  diary  in  my  hand.

Her  hands  shook.  She  kept  touching  the  emblem.  Over  and  over.  
I  haven't  seen  her  look  like  that  in  years.  
 
Then  Arjun  said  —  wait.  Before  anything  else.  I  need  to  say  something.  
"I've  spent  my  entire  life  being  good  at  things.  Boards.  JEE.  The  startup.  Everything  
had
 
a
 
metric.
 
You
 
were
 
the
 
only
 
thing
 
I
 
couldn't
 
measure.
 
And
 
I
 
tried.
 
God
 
knows
 
I
 
tried."
 
I  didn't  say  anything.  
"You  never  made  it  easy.  I  don't  think  you  even  noticed  how  hard  I  was  trying.  Main  
jaanta
 
hoon,
 
Naina.
 
Main
 
always
 
jaanta
 
tha.
 
Par
 
main
 
gaya
 
nahi.
 
Because
 
I
 
didn't
 
want
 
a
 
relationship
 
that
 
was
 
easy.
 
I
 
wanted
 
this
 
one."
 
"Two  years.  Every  version  of  you  —  distracted,  unavailable,  somewhere  else  in  your  
head.
 
I
 
stayed.
 
Because
 
whatever
 
little
 
was
 
there
 
—
 
it
 
was
 
real.
 
And
 
I
 
can
 
wait
 
for
 
real."
 
He  held  out  his  hand.  
"So.  Will  you  marry  me?  Properly.  Knowing  that  I  know  exactly  what  I'm  asking."  
I  looked  at  him.  
Then  I  looked  at  Maa.  
She  was  looking  at  me.  Those  eyes  —  the  ones  she's  had  since  Rohan.  The  ones  that  
ask
 
without
 
asking.
 
Three  years.  Every  thread.  
"Haan,"  I  heard  myself  say.  
Didn't  think  before.  Didn't  think  after.  
Maa  exhaled.  Papa  raised  his  glass.  Arjun  closed  his  eyes  —  just  one  second  —  then  
came
 
back.
 
Composed.
 
Warm.
 
Right.
 
Dinner  continued.  Wine  came.  Maa  held  my  hand.  I  must  have  said  the  right  things.  I  
don't
 
remember
 
exactly
 
what.
 
 
Sitting  here  now.  
I  know  what  I  did.  I  know  why  I  did  it.  
What  I  can't  explain  —  something  else  happened  in  that  same  second.  Underneath  the  
yes.
 
A
 
door.
 
Closing
 
very
 
quietly.
 
The
 
way
 
a
 
door
 
closes
 
when
 
someone
 
has
 
already
 
left
 
and
 
isn't
 
coming
 
back.
 
I  know  that  feeling.  
I  just  didn't  know  it  would  feel  this  final.  
 
Maa  squeezed  my  hand  during  dessert.  Whispered  —  "Khush  hoon,  beta."  
I  said  —  "Main  bhi."  
One  of  those  was  true.  
I  picked  up  my  phone  before  turning  off  the  light.  
His  profile  picture  had  changed.  
The  photo  I  took  this  afternoon.  Afternoon  light.  Blue  shirt.  He's  not  looking  at  the  
camera
 
—
 
he's
 
looking
 
somewhere
 
slightly
 
to
 
the
 
left,
 
the
 
way
 
he
 
always
 
does
 
when
 
he's
 
thinking.
 
I
 
don't
 
know
 
why
 
I
 
noticed
 
that.
 
I
 
did.
 
I  took  that  photo.  Three  hours  before  I  said  yes  to  someone  else.  
I  put  the  phone  face  down  on  the  bed.  
Laptop  off.  Light  off.  
—  N.

 CHAPTER  23  
Fifteen  Minutes  of  Silence  
March  21,  2026  —  Lucknow  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
A.  The  Safety  Officer  |  Morning  
I  opened  the  gate,  and  the  cool  air  immediately  rushed  in.  It  was  the  middle  of  March.  
The
 
sunlight
 
was
 
soft,
 
the
 
wind
 
slightly
 
dry.
 
The  bike  was  parked  in  the  corner.  I  had  just  put  the  key  into  the  ignition  when  I  saw  a  
tiny
 
shadow
 
moving
 
in
 
from
 
the
 
left.
 
"Chacho…"  
Pihu  was  curling  her  way  toward  the  gate.  She  was  in  her  night-suit,  one  sandal  on  
her
 
foot,
 
the
 
other
 
completely
 
bare.
 
"Arre,  madam,"  I  said.  "Poora  pair  pehen  ke  aao."  
She  looked  down  at  her  feet,  then  back  up  at  me.  The  missing  sandal  was  an  entirely  
irrelevant
 
detail
 
to
 
her.
 
"Chacho…  baai…  gumm."  A  direct  demand.  
Bhaiya  was  standing  in  the  doorframe.  His  shirt  was  half-buttoned,  a  tie  hanging  
loosely
 
over
 
his
 
shoulder.
 
"Ek  chakkar,"  he  said  in  a  normal,  even  tone.  "Helmet  ke  saath.  Road  se  bahar  nahi."  
"Ek  hi,"  I  repeated.  
Pihu  had  already  reached  the  bike.  She  tapped  her  small  palm  against  my  helmet.  
"Helet.  Chacho…  helet."  Her  expression  was  dead  serious,  as  if  she  were  reminding  
me
 
of
 
a
 
critical
 
protocol.
 
"Tu  chief  safety  officer  hai,"  I  said,  strapping  the  helmet  on.  
I  made  her  sit  on  the  front  seat.  Both  her  hands  were  placed  near  the  center  of  the  
handle.
 
I
 
held
 
her
 
waist
 
securely
 
with
 
my
 
left
 
hand.
 
Engine
 
on.
 
A
 
slow,
 
steady
 
round
 
around
 
the
 
building
 
premises.
 
The  wind  hit  our  faces.  From  right  in  front  of  me,  I  could  hear  Pihu’s  tiny  voice.  
"Hee  hee…  gumm…  gumm…"  
A  stray  dog  was  sitting  near  the  drain  at  the  corner.  She  waved  her  hand  at  it.  
"Bow  bow…"  
"Bow  bow  ko  bhi  office  jaana  hai,"  I  commented  lightly.  
Two  rounds.  The  brake.  Back  to  the  gate.  
As  I  was  getting  her  off  the  bike,  she  tapped  the  helmet  one  more  time.  
"Firr…  kal  gumm."  
"Dekhenge,"  I  said.  
She  looked  straight  into  my  eyes.  
"Chacho…  aana."  Her  words  were  slightly  clearer  this  time.  
"Try  karunga."  I  offered  a  faint  smile.  
She  ran  back  toward  the  corridor.  "Papa…  chacho  baai  gumm…"  Her  voice  faded  
inside
 
the
 
house.
 
I  took  the  bike  out  onto  the  road.  The  wide  roads  of  Gomti  Nagar.  Office  crowds  on  
scooters,
 
yellow
 
school
 
vans,
 
delivery
 
riders.
 
The
 
plan
 
for
 
the
 
day
 
was
 
simple:
 
the
 
showroom,
 
people,
 
clothes,
 
and
 
standing
 
on
 
my
 
feet
 
until
 
the
 
night.
 
 B.  The  Blue  Rack  |  Afternoon  
The  shutter  of  Heritage  Threads  was  half-raised.  Suresh  saw  me  and  pulled  it  all  the  
way
 
up.
 
"Namaste,  bhaiya."  
"Haan."  I  gave  a  brief  nod.  
Inside,  there  was  the  faint,  sterile  smell  of  the  AC,  fresh  paint,  and  the  crisp  starch  of  
new
 
clothes.
 
Murli
 
was
 
running
 
a
 
cloth
 
over
 
the
 
entrance
 
mirror
 
for
 
the
 
last
 
time.
 
"Subah,"  Anshika  Shukla  called  out  from  the  counter.  Her  hair  was  tied  up  in  a  clip,  
her
 
laptop
 
already
 
booted
 
up.
 
"Subah."  
"Dekha?  Aaj  time  pe  aaye  ho."  
"Traffic  ka  mood  achha  tha,"  I  replied.  
"Blue  rack  tumhare  naam  pe  hai.  Kal  wali  aunty  wapas  aa  sakti  hain,"  she  warned.  
"Hmm."  
The  routine  was  simple.  Straighten  the  tags.  Fold  the  sleeves.  Ensure  all  hangers  face  
the
 
exact
 
same
 
direction.
 
 C.  The  Empty  Floor  |  9:15  PM  
Closing  routine.  
Murli  on  the  mirrors.  Suresh  at  the  gate.  Anshika  closing  down  the  register.  
I  was  at  the  blue  rack—the  final  round.  Checking  tags.  Spacing.  One  hanger  was  
slightly
 
tilted;
 
I
 
corrected
 
it.
 
Rajesh  Sir  was  in  his  cabin,  putting  on  his  coat.  "Eklavya,  sab  theek  hai?"  
"Haan,  Sir."  
"Achha.  Jaao  phir."  
The  staff  filed  out  one  by  one.  Murli  first.  Then  Suresh.  Anshika  locked  the  cash  
counter,
 
picked
 
up
 
her
 
bag,
 
gave
 
me
 
a

unreadable

without
 
saying

and
 
then
 
she
 
was
 
gone

Rajesh  Sir  stepped  out  of  the  cabin.  "Lights  tum  band  kar  dena.  Main  bahar  hoon."  He  
walked
 
toward
 
the
 
glass
 
doors,
 
already
 
talking
 
to
 
someone
 
on
 
his
 
phone.
 
The  showroom  was  almost  empty.  
Almost.  
 D.  The  Fifteen  Minutes  
Naina  was  standing  near  the  center  island.  
She  had  come  down  after  the  staff  had  left.  There  was  no  file  in  her  hand,  no  bag,  no  
official
 
reason.
 
It
 
was
 
just
 
her.
 
She  was  wearing  a  white  kurta.  Her  hair  was  left  open.  She  looked  exactly  the  way  she  
sometimes
 
did
 
on
 
a
 
Sunday
 
morning—when
 
the
 
untouchable
 
corporate
 
armor
 
was
 
left
 
behind
 
at
 
home.
 
She  looked  at  the  ivory  piece—the  one  lakh  forty  thousand  rupee  piece.  She  reached  
out,
 
feeling
 
the
 
texture
 
of
 
the
 
heavy
 
fabric
 
with
 
her
 
fingers.
 
Then
 
she
 
pulled
 
her
 
hand
 
away.
 
I  was  at  the  rack.  Tags.  Spacing.  
She  didn't  look  at  me.  I  didn't  look  at  her.  
But  we  both  knew  the  other  was  there.  
A  few  minutes  passed  exactly  like  that.  
Then,  she  sat  down  near  the  center  island—on  the  low  wooden  ledge  meant  for  
displays.
 
One
 
hand
 
resting
 
on
 
her
 
knee,
 
the
 
other
 
in
 
her
 
lap.
 
I  left  the  rack,  walked  over  to  a  lower  shelf—there  was  a  genuine  adjustment  needed  
there—and
 
sat
 
down
 
on
 
the
 
floor,
 
leaning
 
my
 
back
 
against
 
the
 
wooden
 
panels.
 
It  was  exactly  like  that  evening  in  April  2023.  The  first  season  after  the  café.  The  
library
 
canteen.
 
The
 
bench.
 
We
 
were
 
both
 
occupying
 
the
 
exact
 
same
 
place—sitting
 
separately—but
 
existing
 
in
 
the
 
same
 
shared
 
space.
 
Nobody  spoke  a  word.  
The  showroom  lights  were  half-dimmed  now.  Rajesh  Sir  was  supposed  to  turn  off  the  
main
 
breakers
 
from
 
outside.
 
The
 
low
 
hum
 
of
 
the
 
AC.
 
The
 
distant,

of
 
the

The

three-note

He
 
had
 
gone
 
home.
 
Fifteen  minutes.  
I  only  know  this  because  I  hadn't  been  counting.  But  when  she  finally  spoke,  it  was  
9:32
 
PM,
 
and
 
when
 
she
 
had

it
 
had

9:17
 
PM.
 
I

the
 
math
 
later.
 
 E.  The  Announcement  
"14  April  ko  engagement  hai."  
That  was  it.  
No  preamble.  No  "Mujhe  kuch  batana  tha."  Just  that  single  sentence—delivered  
straight—like
 
something
 
that
 
had
 
been
 
trapped
 
inside
 
for
 
far
 
too
 
many
 
days
 
and
 
simply
 
couldn't
 
be
 
held
 
back
 
anymore.
 
I  kept  my  back  pressed  against  the  shelf.  I  didn't  move  a  single  muscle.  
The  ivory  piece  was  still  sitting  there  on  the  center  island  above  us.  One  lakh  forty  
thousand.
 
Fourteen
 
people.
 
Six
 
months.
 
Rajesh
 
Sir
 
had
 
told
 
me
 
the
 
stats
 
on
 
my
 
first
 
day.
 
It
 
was
 
still
 
right
 
there.
 
"Arjun  ke  saath?"  I  asked  quietly.  
"Haan."  
A  one-second  pause.  
"Sach  mein  ready  ho?"  
She  stayed  quiet.  
I  didn't  need  an  answer.  She  knew  that.  I  knew  that  too.  But  the  question  existed  
because
 
it
 
was
 
necessary
 
for
 
the
 
question
 
to
 
exist.
 
Some
 
things
 
are
 
spoken
 
simply
 
so
 
they
 
can
 
hang
 
in
 
the
 
air—so
 
that
 
someone
 
hears
 
them
 
out
 
loud
 
at
 
least
 
once.
 
She  kept  looking  at  the  ivory  piece.  
I  kept  looking  at  the  floor  tiles.  
The  AC  kept  humming.  The  traffic  kept  moving  further  away.  
Then:  
"Pata  nahi."  
Just  that.  
"Sach  mein  nahi  pata."  
 
F.  The  Departure  
I  started  to  stand  up.  
"Eklavya."  
I  stopped.  
She  still  wasn't  looking  at  me.  Her  eyes  were  fixed  on  the  ivory  piece.  Always  the  ivory  
piece.
 
"Kuch  nahi.  Bas  —  ruk  jao  ek  second."  
One  second.  
Two.  
Then,  she  stood  up.  She  picked  up  her  bag—I  didn't  even  know  when  she  had  set  it  
down.
 
She
 
smoothed
 
out
 
the
 
creases
 
on
 
her
 
white
 
kurta.
 
"Rajesh  ji  bahar  hain.  Gate  lock  karna  hoga."  The  business  tone.  Exactly  that.  
"Goodnight."
 
She  walked  toward  the  glass  doors.  She  placed  one  hand  on  the  handle.  She  
stopped—just
 
for
 
a
 
fraction
 
of
 
a
 
heartbeat,
 
just
 
one—and
 
then
 
pulled
 
it
 
open.
 
She  stepped  outside.  The  sharp  click  of  her  footsteps  fading.  
The  heavy  engine  of  the  SUV  roaring  to  life.  
A  left  turn  at  the  signal.  
Gone.  
 G.  The  Aftermath  
I  stood  there  in  the  silence.  
The  showroom  was  left  with  only  the  hum  of  the  AC,  the  ivory  piece,  and  the  
half-dimmed
 
lights.
 
From  outside,  Rajesh  Sir's  voice  called  out:  "Eklavya,  sab  theek?"  
"Haan  Sir.  Aa  raha  hoon."  
I  turned  off  the  remaining  lights.  Locked  the  glass  gate.  Stepped  out  into  the  night.  
The  Lucknow  night—the  vehicles,  the  people,  the  yellow  streetlights.  
Everything  was  exactly  where  it  had  always  been.  
 INTERLUDE  
Naina's  Diary  |  March  21,  2026  |  Night  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
I  had  to  go  today.  
There  was  no  other  reason.  No  file,  no  inventory  review.  I  just  had  to  go.  I  just  had  to  
tell
 
him.
 
Because
 
if
 
I
 
didn't—if
 
he
 
heard
 
it
 
from
 
someone
 
else,
 
or
 
if
 
he
 
saw
 
it
 
himself
 
on
 
April
 
14th—it
 
would
 
have
 
been
 
different.
 
I
 
didn't
 
know
 
this
 
before.
 
Today,
 
I
 
knew.
 
The  staff  left.  Rajesh  ji  stepped  outside.  I  stayed  back  near  the  center  island.  
He  was  at  the  rack.  I  was  staring  at  the  ivory  piece.  
We  were  both  just  there.  Neither  of  us  said  a  word.  
Fifteen  minutes.  
I  don't  know  how  those  fifteen  minutes  passed.  But  they  did.  
And  then  I  spoke.  
"14  April  ko  engagement  hai."  
He  stayed  quiet.  
Exactly  the  way  he  always  stays  quiet—that  specific  silence  that  processes  things,  
that
 
doesn't
 
judge,
 
that
 
simply...
 
listens.
 
He
 
listens.
 
"Arjun  ke  saath?"  he  asked.  
"Haan."  
A  one-second  silence.  
"Sach  mein  ready  ho?"  
That  question.  
That  exact  question.  
Three  years  ago,  sitting  on  a  bench  in  the  park,  he  had  asked  me—"Upset  ho  ya  
irritated?"—and
 
those
 
two
 
simple
 
words
 
had
 
shattered
 
my
 
entire
 
script.
 
Today,
 
it
 
was
 
just
 
one
 
question.
 
Just
 
one.
 
"Sach  mein  ready  ho?"  
No  lecture.  No  "Yeh  sahi  nahi  hai."  No  imposition  of  his  own  opinion.  Just  that  
question—direct,
 
stripped
 
of
 
any
 
extra
 
weight,
 
exactly
 
what
 
needed
 
to
 
be
 
asked.
 
And  me—I  didn't  know  the  answer.  
I  don't  know  why  I  expected  that  I  would  know.  I  thought  that  when  he  finally  asked,  
something
 
would
 
rise
 
up
 
from
 
deep
 
inside

a
 
resounding

or
 
a
 
definitive
 
no—and
 
I
 
would
 
finally
 
know
 
the
 
truth.
 
Nothing  rose  up.  
Just  that  same  flat,  suffocating  silence  that  has  been  living  inside  me  since  March  
18th.
 
"Pata  nahi.  Sach  mein  nahi  pata."  
He  started  to  get  up.  
I  told  him  to  stop.  
Why?  I  don't  know.  There  was  no  follow-up  sentence.  There  was  no  logical  reason.  I  
just
 
needed
 
him
 
to
 
stay
 
there
 
for
 
one
 
more
 
second.
 
Just
 
that.
 
One  second.  Two.  
Then  I  stood  up.  Bag.  Kurta.  Gate.  
"Goodnight."  
I  sat  in  my  car  for  a  full  minute.  
I  didn't  start  the  engine.  
He  had  asked—"Sach  mein  ready  ho?"  
And  I  had  said—"Pata  nahi."  
This  was  the  answer  I  should  have  given  to  Arjun.  This  was  the  answer  I  should  have  
given
 
to
 
Dr.
 
Mehta.
 
But
 
instead,
 
I
 
gave
 
it—inside
 
a
 
half-lit
 
showroom,
 
standing
 
in
 
front
 
of
 
an
 
ivory
 
piece—to
 
him.
 
Because  he  asks  when  he  needs  to  ask.  And  he  stays  quiet  when  he  needs  to  stay  
quiet.
 
And  me—I  know  what  I  should  do.  Everyone  knows  what  they  should  do.  What  we  
want
 
to
 
do
 
is
 
an
 
entirely
 
different
 
thing.
 
The  two  don't  always  exist  together.  
I  started  the  engine.  
Left  turn  at  the  signal.  
—  N.

INTERLUDE  
Naina's  Diary  |  March  22  –  24,  2026  
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
March  22  |  Night  
Arjun  planned  a  dinner  tonight.  The  Taj.  The  exact  same  place.  
He  was  trying  so  hard.  Dal  makhani.  No  butter  naan.  Sparkling  water.  He  remembered  
every
 
single
 
preference
 
perfectly.
 
But  I  wasn't  there.  
I  mean—I  was.  Physically.  I  was  sitting  in  the  chair,  my  hands  were  resting  on  the  
table,
 
my
 
eyes
 
were
 
directed
 
at
 
him.
 
But
 
I
 
wasn't
 
actually
 
there.
 
He  kept  talking—the  Q2  expansion,  the  follow-up  for  Maa's  kurta  at  the  National  
Museum,
 
playing
 
golf
 
with
 
Papa.
 
Everything
 
was
 
organized.
 
Everything
 
was
 
thoughtful.
 
Everything
 
was
 
exactly
 
the
 
way
 
it
 
was
 
supposed
 
to
 
be.
 
I  nodded.  I  smiled  exactly  when  I  was  supposed  to  smile.  
His  hand  reached  across  the  table  and  held  mine.  It  was  warm.  It  was  deliberate.  
I  looked  at  it  and  thought  to  myself:  This  is  a  hand.  This  is  a  table.  This  is  a  restaurant.  
Everything
 
is
 
in
 
exactly
 
the
 
right
 
place.
 
I  am  the  one  in  the  wrong  place.  
Then  I  silently  scolded  myself—stop  thinking  like  this.  Arjun  is  genuinely  a  good  man.  
What
 
he
 
did
 
for
 
Maa
 
was
 
incredibly
 
real.
 
Being
 
'good'
 
and
 
being
 
'present'
 
are
 
two
 
entirely
 
different
 
things—but
 
presence
 
can
 
be
 
learned.
 
It
 
just
 
takes
 
time.
 
But  the  entire  three  hours  of  that  dinner  were  spent  desperately  trying  to  convince  
myself
 
of
 
that.
 
 March  23  |  Afternoon  
I  didn't  go  to  the  showroom  today.  
It  was  intentional.  
There  was  work—Q2  projections,  the  March-end  audit,  reviewing  files  with  Rajesh  ji.  
But
 
I
 
didn't
 
go
 
to
 
Gomti
 
Nagar.
 
I
 
sat
 
in
 
the
 
main
 
office
 
and
 
did
 
everything
 
from

Over
 
the
 
phone.
 
Over
 
email.
 
Rajesh  ji  called  in  the  afternoon.  
"Ma'am,  aaoge  aaj?"  
"Nahi.  Files  bhej  do."  
That  was  it.  
 March  24  |  Morning  
Session  with  Dr.  Mehta.  
She  sat  across  from  me  and  asked,  "Kya  chal  raha  hai?"  
I  told  her.  The  engagement.  Arjun.  The  inability  to  remain  present  at  dinner.  
She  kept  listening,  taking  her  notes.  
Then  she  stopped,  looked  over  her  glasses,  and  asked:  "Aur  kya  chal  raha  hai?"  
"Bas  yahi,"  I  replied.  
"Naina."

He  refused.

The  Crowd  and  the  Zero  April  13,  2026  —  Gomti  Nagar,  Lucknow  |  9:30  PM

jise
 
ahista
 
ahista
 
solve
 
karne
 
mein
 
maza
 
aata
 
hai.
 
Khubsurat
 
hai."
 
Anshika  rolled  her  eyes.  "Aap  logo  ki  philosophical  baatein  mere  hostel  ke  dimaag  ke  
upar
 
se
 
jaa
 
rahi
 
hain.
 
Kheer
 
achhi
 
thi
 
waise."
 
Anshika  packed  her  plastic  dabba  and  hopped  off  the  counter.  "Main  floor  pe  jaa  rahi  
hoon.
 
Aap
 
log
 
apni
 
khubsurat
 
mystery
 
solve
 
karo."
 
The  door  clicked  shut.  
It  was  just  the  two  of  us.  
She  didn't  say  anything  immediately.  She  just  took  the  last  piece  of  cucumber  from  
my
 
box.
 
"Kal  Wednesday  hai,"  she  said.  Not  looking  at  me.  
"Haan.  Showroom  band  rahega.  Pura  din  chhutti."  
"Main  Pihu  se  milne  aaungi."  
I  looked  at  her.  
"Aise  hi,"  she  added.  "Bas  milne."  
She  stood  up,  patted  my  shoulder  lightly—a  gesture  so  natural  I  barely  registered  the  
touch
 
until
 
her
 
hand
 
was
 
gone—and
 
walked
 
out.
 
INTERLUDE  VIII  
Naina's  Diary  
April  14,  2026  —  Late  Night  
I  went  to  Arjun's  house  this  morning.  
He  was  in  his  study,  packing  some  files.  He  saw  me  at  the  door,  saw  my  face,  and  
before
 
I
 
could
 
say
 
a
 
word,
 
he
 
smiled.
 
A
 
very
 
tired,
 
very
 
knowing
 
smile.
 
"Tum  sach  mein  Eklavya  ban  sakti  ho?"  he  asked  quietly.  
"Nahi,"  I  said.  "Aur  tum  bhi  nahi  ban  sakte.  Main  hamesha  ek  performance  deti  
rahungi,
 
aur
 
tum
 
hamesha
 
us
 
ek
 
insaan
 
se
 
compete
 
karte
 
rahoge
 
jo
 
race
 
mein
 
hai
 
hi
 
nahi.
 
Eklavya
 
hamesha
 
se
 
meri
 
duniya
 
ka
 
part
 
tha,
 
aur
 
rahega.
 
Uske
 
bagair
 
main
 
poori
 
nahi
 
hoon."
 
Arjun  just  nodded.  He  didn't  argue.  He  is  too  smart,  too  logical  to  fight  for  a  losing  
trade.
 
"Mujhe  pata  tha,"  he  told  me.  "Main  kal  raat  hi  samajh  gaya  tha  jab  tumne  sach  bola.  
Goldman
 
Sachs
 
ka
 
jo
 
offer
 
hai
 
London
 
ka,
 
main
 
accept
 
kar
 
raha
 
hoon.
 
As
 
an
 
investor,
 
main
 
Heritage
 
Threads
 
mein
 
rahunga.
 
Ek
 
dost
 
ki
 
tarah."
 
It  ended  just  like  that.  No  drama.  No  tears.  He  has  his  world,  and  he  will  conquer  it.  He  
was
 
just
 
trying
 
to
 
win
 
the
 
wrong
 
game
 
here.
 
When  I  came  back  home,  Maa  was  in  the  kitchen.  I  told  her  the  engagement  is  off.  
She  didn't  gasp.  She  didn't  scold  me.  She  just  stopped  what  she  was  doing,  walked  
over,
 
and
 
held
 
my
 
hand
 
tightly.
 
Just
 
that.
 
It
 
was
 
enough.
 
Papa  found  out  an  hour  later.  We  still  don't  talk  face-to-face.  I  am  the  one  who  keeps  
the
 
distance,
 
the
 
anger
 
from
 
the
 
past
 
still
 
sitting
 
somewhere
 
inside
 
me.
 
He
 
didn't
 
come
 
to
 
my
 
room.
 
My
 
phone
 
buzzed
 
instead.
 
"Your  life.  Your  decision.  I  respect  it."  
He  tries,  in  his  own  awkward  way.  I'm  the  one  who  doesn't  know  how  to  bridge  that  
gap
 
yet.
 
In  the  afternoon,  I  went  to  the  showroom.  I  went  to  him.  
I  crashed  his  lunch.  I  ate  his  food.  Anshika  was  there,  calling  him  'bro'  and  teasing  
him
 
about
 
the
 
Instagram
 
photo
 
I
 
made
 
him
 
upload.
 
The
 
whole
 
thing
 
was
 
so...
 
light.
 
So
 
incredibly
 
normal.
 
I  brought  up  Sivi.  I  used  Anshika  to  ask  him  how  he  was  with  her.  
He  said:  "Koi  boundary  nahi  thi.  Jab  the,  toh  bas  the."  
And  suddenly,  every  mistake  I  ever  made  with  him  over  the  last  three  years  made  
sense.
 
He  has  my  old  diary  from  2023.  I  can't  read  those  pages  physically  anymore,  but  I  
don't
 
need
 
to.
 
I
 
remember
 
every
 
plan
 
I
 
wrote
 
in
 
it.
 
The
 
cafes
 
in
 
Koramangala.
 
The
 
trips
 
to  the  mountains.  The  books  I  would  make  him  read.  I  was  always  trying  to  drag  him  
into
 
my
 
world.
 
I  was  wrong.  You  can't  put  boundaries  and  plans  on  someone  who  breathes  freely  
only
 
when
 
he
 
is
 
let
 
go.
 
I  don’t  need  to  drag  him  into  my  world.  
Main  uski  duniya  mein  jaaungi.  Bina  us  par  bojh  daale.  Bas  dekhungi  ki  uski  duniya  
kaisi
 
hai.
 
Anshika  called  him  a  Netflix  mystery  today.  I  told  her  it's  a  beautiful  one.  
Because  it  is.  
Kal  Wednesday  hai.  Showroom  band  rehta  hai.  
Main  Pihu  se  milne  uske  ghar  jaungi.  Ek  'Owner  Ma'am'  ki  tarah  nahi.  Ek  dost  ki  tarah.  
Ek
 
aam
 
insaan
 
ki
 
tarah.
 
Let's  see  what  happens  when  there  are  no  boundaries.  
—  N.  
***  
 
#  CHAPTER  31  
##  The  Weight  of  Water  
###  April  15,  2026  —  Lucknow  
 
Wednesday.  The  showroom  was  closed.  
 
It  was  just  past  two  in  the  afternoon.  Bhabhi  was  in  the  kitchen.  I  was  lying  on  my  
back
 
on
 
the
 
living
 
room
 
carpet.
 
 
Pihu  was  resting  on  my  chest  and  stomach,  completely  still,  like  something  delicate  
that
 
had
 
found
 
the
 
right
 
place
 
to
 
settle.
 
She
 
liked
 
sleeping
 
on
 
people
 
this
 
way.
 
Her
 
small,
 
light
 
weight
 
pressed
 
into
 
me
 
with
 
a
 
warmth
 
that
 
asked
 
for
 
nothing.
 
She
 
breathed
 
slowly,
 
rising
 
and
 
falling
 
with
 
my
 
chest.
 
 
She  had  her  Ram  Lala  toy  in  one  hand.  She  kept  bringing  it  close  to  my  face,  then  
suddenly
 
pressing
 
her
 
small
 
mouth
 
against
 
my
 
cheek.
 
Her
 
new
 
teeth.
 
Her
 
wet
 
lips.
 
A
 
clumsy,
 
uncoordinated
 
baby
 
kiss.
 
She
 
didn't
 
know
 
what
 
it
 
meant,
 
but
 
she
 
liked
 
the
 
contact.
 
She
 
would
 
press
 
her
 
mouth
 
and
 
teeth
 
against
 
my
 
face,
 
make
 
a
 
small
 
happy
 
sound,
 
and
 
then
 
put
 
her
 
head
 
back
 
down
 
on
 
my
 
chest.
 
The
 
pleasure
 
was
 
entirely
 
hers.
 
The
 
warmth
 
was
 
entirely
 
mine.
 
 
I  placed  both  hands  on  her  small  back,  steadying  her.  
 
The  doorbell  rang.  
 
Bhabhi  came  out  of  the  kitchen  and  opened  the  door.  
 
Naina  was  standing  there.  Faded  blue  jeans.  An  oversized  white  shirt,  sleeves  folded  
to
 
the
 
elbow.
 
No
 
sunglasses.
 
No
 
sharp
 
manager's
 
look.
 
 
She  stepped  inside.  Her  footsteps  stopped.  
 
She  was  looking  at  the  carpet.  At  Pihu  lying  on  my  chest  without  any  fear,  pressing  
her
 
mouth
 
against
 
my
 
face
 
every
 
few
 
minutes.
 
Something
 
settled
 
in
 
Naina's
 
expression
 
—
 
the
 
way
 
a
 
person
 
looks
 
when
 
they
 
have
 
been
 
moving
 
for
 
a
 
long
 
time
 
and
 
have
 
suddenly
 
found
 
shade.
 
 
She  came  forward  quietly  and  sat  cross-legged  on  the  carpet  beside  us.  She  ran  her  
hand
 
lightly
 
over
 
Pihu's
 
back.
 
Pihu
 
lifted
 
her
 
head,
 
looked
 
at
 
her,
 
and
 
held
 
the
 
torn
 
Ram
 
Lala
 
toy
 
out
 
toward
 
her.
 
 
"Isko  naya  chahiye,"  Naina  said,  touching  Pihu's  cheek  gently.  Her  voice  carried  the  
same
 
quiet
 
that
 
was
 
already
 
spread
 
across
 
the
 
room.
 
Then
 
she
 
looked
 
at
 
me.
 
"Mujhe
 
bhi
 
chalna
 
hai.
 
Tumhare
 
aur
 
Pihu
 
ke
 
saath."
 
 
Bhabhi  came  back  with  two  cups  of  chai.  She  smiled  when  she  saw  Naina  —  naturally,  
without
 
ceremony.
 
 
"Achha  hai  aa  gayi,"  she  said,  setting  the  cups  on  the  table.  "Aise  bhi  hum  19  tarikh  
ko
 
Ayodhya
 
jaane
 
ka
 
soch
 
rahe
 
the.
 
Chaturthi
 
hai
 
us
 
din.
 
Bada
 
snan
 
hota
 
hai."
 
 
Naina  handed  the  toy  back  to  Pihu.  "Chaturthi?"  
 
I  sat  up  slowly,  settling  Pihu  in  my  lap,  and  picked  up  the  chai.  
 
"Haan,"  I  said.  "Par  sirf  snan  nahi  hota.  Wahan  ek  aur  process  hota  hai.  Ghade  ka."  
 
Naina  picked  up  her  cup.  There  was  a  question  in  her  eyes,  but  she  didn't  rush  it.  She  
gave
 
me
 
the
 
space
 
to
 
take
 
my
 
time.
 
 
"Log  rote  hain,  depression  mein  jaate  hain,  par  kuch  grief  aise  hote  hain  jo  bahar  nahi  
aate,"
 
I
 
began.
 
"Woh
 
andar
 
baith
 
jaate
 
hain.
 
Ek
 
ruke
 
hue,
 
stagnant
 
paani
 
ki
 
tarah.
 
The
 
mind
 
cannot
 
process
 
abstract
 
grief.
 
It
 
needs
 
a
 
physical
 
form
 
—
 
something
 
to
 
hold,
 
and
 
something
 
to
 
release."
 
 
Naina's  grip  on  her  cup  tightened.  I  knew  who  she  was  thinking  about.  Nandini  ji.  Her  
mother.
 
The
 
woman
 
who
 
had
 
been
 
carrying
 
that
 
stillness
 
inside
 
her
 
like
 
a
 
lake
 
with
 
no
 
outlet,
 
since
 
the
 
day
 
Rohan
 
left.
 
 
"Isliye  mitti  ka  ghada  use  karte  hain,"  I  said,  my  fingers  moving  through  Pihu's  hair.  
"The
 
pot
 
is
 
filled
 
with
 
water.
 
That
 
water
 
is
 
everything
 
you
 
have
 
been
 
holding.
 
When
 
you
 
set
 
it
 
into
 
the
 
Sarayu
 
—
 
into
 
the
 
moving
 
current
 
—
 
it
 
joins
 
the
 
flow.
 
Yeh
 
sirf
 
religion
 
nahi
 
hai
 
Naina.
 
It's
 
a
 
deeply
 
scientific,
 
psychological
 
trigger.
 
The
 
brain
 
physically
 
watches
 
the
 
thing
 
that
 
was
 
frozen
 
take
 
a
 
new
 
shape
 
and
 
move.
 
The
 
burden
 
transfers."
 
 
The  room  went  entirely  quiet.  
 
Pihu  sat  still  in  my  lap.  Naina  held  the  cup  in  both  hands,  exactly  where  she  was.  Her  
eyes
 
had
 
gone
 
slightly
 
wet,
 
but
 
she
 
wasn't
 
crying.
 
She
 
was
 
just
 
—
 
feeling
 
the
 
weight
 
of
 
the
 
idea.
 
 
"Maa,"  she  said.  One  word.  Very  quietly.  
 
"Unhe  zaroorat  hai  us  flow  ki,"  I  said.  "Woh  Rohan  ko  bhoolengi  nahi.  Par  woh  us  
stagnant
 
paani
 
se
 
bahar
 
aa
 
jayengi."
 
 
Naina  took  a  long  breath.  "Hum  unhe  le  jayenge.  19  ko."  
 
She  looked  at  me.  There  was  no  formality  in  it.  Just  a  very  deep,  settled  
understanding.
 
 
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
 
A  while  later,  Bhabhi  took  Naina  to  the  kitchen  to  feed  her.  
 
I  carried  Pihu  to  my  room  and  put  her  down  to  sleep.  Straightened  the  books  on  the  
desk.
 
 
Then  I  heard  footsteps.  
 
Naina  was  at  the  doorway.  She  didn't  ask  to  come  in,  but  she  didn't  walk  straight  in  
either.
 
She
 
looked
 
at
 
the
 
room
 
first.
 
 
Then  she  stepped  inside.  
 
Her  eyes  went  to  the  rough  chart  on  the  wall.  Then  to  my  desk.  
 
The  notebook  was  lying  open.  The  first  draft  of  Viram.  A  riverbank.  A  few  benches.  An  
empty
 
space
 
in
 
the
 
center.
 
 
She  came  to  the  desk.  Her  hands  were  clasped  behind  her  back.  She  just  looked  at  
the
 
drawing.
 
Felt
 
what
 
was
 
on
 
that
 
paper
 
—
 
the
 
stillness
 
I
 
had
 
sketched
 
—
 
without
 
asking
 
what
 
it
 
was
 
for.
 
 
Then  her  gaze  shifted  slightly  to  the  side.  
 
The  diary  was  there.  
 
The  one  she  had  given  me  in  January  2025.  
 
Her  hands  came  forward.  She  picked  it  up.  It  didn't  look  untouched.  The  leather  at  the  
edges
 
had
 
worn
 
soft.
 
The
 
pages
 
were
 
slightly
 
curved,
 
the
 
way
 
pages
 
curve
 
when
 
they
 
have
 
been
 
opened
 
many
 
times,
 
when
 
time
 
has
 
been
 
spent
 
with
 
them.
 
 
Naina's  hands  stilled  on  the  cover.  Her  eyes  closed.  
 
She  remembered  the  day  she  had  placed  it  in  my  hands.  Her  voice  that  day,  slightly  
unsteady
 
—
 
 
*"Jab  lage  ki  meri  yaadein  dhundhli  ho  rahi  hain,  tab  isse  padh  lena.  Mujhe  bhoolna  
mat
 
Eklavya
।
"*
 
 
She  didn't  turn  a  single  page.  The  wear  on  the  cover  had  already  told  her  everything  
she
 
needed
 
to
 
know.
 
 
She  pressed  the  diary  against  her  chest.  Took  a  slow  breath  and  released  it.  One  tear  
came
 
loose
 
and
 
moved
 
down
 
her
 
cheek,
 
but
 
her
 
face
 
held
 
a
 
quiet
 
that
 
didn't
 
belong
 
to
 
grief.
 
 
"Tumne  mujhe  bhoolne  nahi  diya,"  she  said,  without  turning  around.  The  weight  in  her  
voice
 
settled
 
into
 
the
 
room
 
and
 
stayed
 
there.
 
 
I  didn't  say  anything.  There  was  nothing  that  needed  to  be  said.  
 
She  turned.  Set  the  diary  back  on  the  desk,  carefully,  with  both  hands.  
 
"19  ko  chalte  hain,"  she  said.  Her  eyes  held  both  the  tear  and  the  peace  at  the  same  
time.
 
"Maa
 
ko
 
us
 
behti
 
nadi
 
ki
 
zaroorat
 
hai.
 
Aur
 
mujhe...
 
is
 
thehraav
 
ki
।
"
 
 
━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━  
 
##  INTERLUDE  IX  
###  Naina's  Diary  
###  April  15,  2026  —  Night  
 
I  went  into  his  room  today.  
 
I  saw  his  notebook  on  the  desk.  A  draft  of  something  he  calls  Viram.  I  didn't  ask  him  
what
 
it
 
was.
 
I
 
just
 
looked
 
at
 
it.
 
It
 
looks
 
like
 
a
 
pause.
 
A
 
place
 
where
 
you
 
don't
 
have
 
to
 
become
 
anything.
 
You
 
just
 
exist.
 
 
And  then  I  saw  the  diary.  
 
The  one  I  gave  him  in  January  2025.  
 
It  wasn't  sitting  there  untouched.  It  looked  worn  out.  The  edges  of  the  pages  were  
soft,
 
the
 
leather
 
had
 
the
 
marks
 
of
 
being
 
held
 
over
 
and
 
over
 
again.
 
 
*"Jab
 
meri
 
yaadein
 
kam
 
hone
 
lagein,
 
toh
 
isse
 
padh
 
lena.
 
Mujhe
 
bhoolna
 
mat
।
"*
 
 
That's  what  I  told  him  when  I  gave  it  to  him.  I  was  so  scared  that  he  would  move  on,  
that
 
my
 
face
 
would
 
fade
 
from
 
his
 
memory
 
in
 
this
 
fast-moving
 
world.
 
 
He  didn't  let  it.  
 
I  had  spent  three  years  thinking  I  was  the  only  one  holding  this  thing  together.  But  he  
was
 
sitting
 
in
 
that
 
room,
 
with
 
my
 
words,
 
keeping
 
me
 
alive
 
in
 
a
 
place
 
the
 
world
 
couldn't
 
reach.
 
 
Mujhe  lagta  tha  main  akele  us  rishte  ko  bacha  rahi  hoon.  Par  woh  is  kamre  mein  baith  
kar,
 
meri
 
likhi
 
hui
 
yaadon
 
ke
 
sahare,
 
mujhe
 
khud
 
mein
 
zinda
 
rakhe
 
hue
 
tha
।
 
 
On  the  19th,  we  are  taking  Maa  to  Ayodhya.  
 
She  needs  to  let  Rohan's  memories  mix  with  the  flowing  river.  
 
But  looking  at  my  old  diary  on  his  desk  today  —  I  know  I  am  never  going  to  let  
Eklavya
 
go.
 
He
 
is
 
my
 
river.
 
And
 
I
 
have
 
finally
 
stepped
 
into
 
the
 
water.
 
 
—  N.
